Divine Attributes
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Philosophers' Objection to Divine Attributes
Sanusi refutes the philosophers' argument against divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' argument that divine attributes would entail dependency and contingency, using the method of denying the entailment and clarifying the meaning of dependency.

Core Idea
Dependency does not always imply contingency; concomitant existence does not contradict necessity.

This lesson presents Sanusi's refutation of a key philosophical objection to divine attributes. The philosophers argued that if attributes existed, they would depend on the Essence, implying contingency. Sanusi responds by denying the entailment and exposing the ambiguity in their use of 'dependency'. You will learn the logical distinction between causal dependency and mere inseparability.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the opening passage where the philosophers present their case: attributes must depend on the Essence because they cannot subsist by themselves, and some are conditions for others, so dependency contradicts necessary existence.
Notice that this resembles the Mu'tazilite argument mentioned earlier.
2
Identify the author's initial response
Look for the phrase 'والجواب : منع الملازمة' (The answer: denial of the entailment). The author rejects that dependency necessarily leads to contingency.
This is a common kalam technique - challenging the logical connection between premises and conclusion.
3
Understand the nuance about dependency
The author distinguishes two meanings of dependency: (1) causal dependency implying need for an external cause, and (2) mere inseparable coexistence (التلازم). The latter does not negate necessity.
The author argues that if by 'dependency' they mean mere reliance without causal need, then it doesn't contradict wajib al-wujud.
4
See the author's concluding critique
The author concludes by accusing the philosophers of sophistry, using the ambiguous term 'dependency' to deceive, and relying on weak imaginations rather than sound reason.
He quotes Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani criticizing Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for accepting this flawed argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ الفلاسفة قدِ احتجّتْ على نفْي الصفاتِ بما يَقرُبُ مِنْ شبهة المعتزلة السابقة ، فقالوا : لو وُجِدَتِ الصفاتُ لَلزِمَ أَنْ تَكونَ مفتقرةً إلى الذاتِ ؛ لاستحالة قيام الصفة بنفسها ، ولأنَّ بعضَها شرط في الباقي ؛ كالحياة التي هي شرط في القدرة والعلم والإرادة ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ المشروط مفتقراً إلى الشرط ومتأخّراً عنه في العقل والافتقار ينافي الوجوبَ ؛ إذ الواجب مستغن على الإطلاق ، وذلكَ منافٍ للافتقار ، والحاجةُ والتقدم على واجب الوجودِ محال
والجواب : منع الملازمة ؛ فإنَّ الافتقار إلى الغيرِ يقتضي أنَّ المفتقِرَ يفيده الغير الوجود ، فيكون حادثاً ، ونحن لا ندعي ذلك ، بل نقولُ : إِنَّ صفاتِهِ تعالى كلَّها واجبة الوجودِ ، غنية عن المقتضي بإطلاق .
وإن عنيتُم بالافتقار الملازمة وعدم انفكاك أحد الوجودين عنِ منعنا الاستثنائية ، ولم يكن الافتقار بهذا المعنى ينافي الوجوب ، فلمَ قلتُم : إنَّ هذا التوقف في العلم أو الوجودِ الذي سمَّيْتُموهُ أنتم افتقاراً .. ينافي وجوب الوجودِ أو يستلزم الإمكان ؟! فإِنَّ الإمكانَ إنَّما يتحقَّقُ بصحة الارتفاع ، وإذا كانا واجبين فلا يصح في العقل ارتفاعُهما ولا ارتفاع أحدهما ، فلا إمكان ولا احتياج لكلِّ منهما ، فاتركوا إذاً عنا لفظ الافتقار والإمكان الموهمين لما تقرَّرَتِ استحالتُهُ مِنَ الاحتياج إلى المؤثر ، وقولوا : كلُّ موجودين متلازمينِ لا يصح في العقلِ ارتفاعُهما ولا ارتفاعُ أحدهما .. فَفَرْضُ وجودِهما محال ! أو قولوا : لا يمكن ثبوتُ واجب يلازمه واجبٌ آخرُ ، أو : لا يصح ثبوتُ واجب إلا خالياً عن واجـب آخر ! وحينئذ تبـدو فضيحتكم بادعائكم ما لا تجدونَ إلى تصحيحه سبيلاً سوى المغالطة بلفظ الافتقار المُوهِم واستعماله لمطلق التوقفِ ، ومطلق التوقف لا يقتضي الحاجة إلى المؤثرِ إلا إذا صح النفي عقلاً ، لا تقديراً في الخيالِ ، أو خطوراً بالبال ؛ كما تخطرُ المستحيلاتُ عند إعراض العقل عن وجه استحالتها
وبالجملة : فالقومُ حَكَموا التخيُّلاتِ على ضعفها ، وجعلوها أدلَّةً فيما لا يهتدي في فيج صحرائِهِ الصعبة المسالك إلا العقل النافذ المؤيَّد بهدايته تعالى
قالَ شرف الدين بنُ التِّلِمْساني : ( ولما اعتقد الفخرُ صحة هذهِ الحُجَّةِ - يعني : شبهة الفلاسفة في أنَّ الافتقار بمعنى مطلق التوقُفِ
And know: The philosophers have argued for the negation of the attributes with something close to the previous argument of the Mu'tazila. They said: If attributes existed, they would necessarily be dependent upon the Essence, because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist by itself, and because some of them are a condition for the rest—like life which is a condition for power, knowledge, and will. So the conditioned must be dependent upon the condition and posterior to it in intellect, and dependency contradicts necessary existence, for the Necessary is independent absolutely, and that is contrary to dependency, and need and priority over the Necessary Being are impossible. The answer: Denial of the entailment. For dependency upon another would necessitate that the dependent receives existence from the other, so it would be contingent. And we do not claim that. Rather, we say: All His attributes, exalted is He, are necessary of existence, absolutely independent of any cause. And if you mean by dependency the inseparability and non-disjunction of one existence from another, then we deny the conditional. And dependency in this sense does not contradict necessity. So why do you say that this reliance in knowledge or existence, which you have called dependency, contradicts necessary existence or necessitates contingency? For contingency is only realized by the possibility of non-existence. And if both are necessary, then it is rationally impossible for both to cease or for one to cease. So there is no contingency and no need for each of them. Therefore, leave aside for us the term dependency and contingency, which imply something whose impossibility has been established—namely, need for an efficient cause. And say: Every two concomitant existences such that it is rationally impossible for both to cease or for one to cease... then the assumption of their existence is impossible! Or say: It is not possible to establish a necessary being that is concomitant with another necessary being. Or: It is not valid to establish a necessary being except that it is free from another necessary being. Then your disgrace becomes apparent by your claiming what you have no way to correct except by sophistry through the misleading term 'dependency' and its use for absolute reliance. And absolute reliance does not entail need for an efficient cause except when non-existence is rationally valid, not when it is estimated in the imagination or occurs to the mind, like impossible things occur to the mind when the intellect turns away from the aspect of their impossibility. In summary: The people [the philosophers] have judged the imaginations despite their weakness, and made them as proofs in a field whose difficult paths are not traversed except by a penetrating intellect supported by His guidance, exalted is He. Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani said: 'When Fakhr [al-Din al-Razi] believed in the soundness of this argument—meaning the philosophers' argument that dependency in the sense of absolute reliance...'
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' argument against the divine attributes. The author first presents the philosophers' objection: if attributes existed, they would be dependent on the Essence (since attributes cannot subsist by themselves) and some attributes (like life) are conditions for others (like power), so the conditioned depends on the condition, and dependency contradicts the necessary existence of God, who is absolutely independent. The author's answer begins by denying the entailment (man' al-mulāzama). He argues that dependency on another would imply the dependent receives existence from that other, making it contingent, but the attributes of God are not contingent—they are necessary existents, independent of any cause. The author then addresses a nuance: if by 'dependency' the philosophers mean mere inseparability or concomitant existence (not actual need for a cause), then it does not contradict necessity. He challenges them to show why such concomitant reliance implies contingency or need for a cause. He explains that contingency requires the possibility of non-existence; if two things are both necessary, it is impossible for either to cease, so there is no contingency or need. He accuses the philosophers of sophistry by using the term 'dependency' ambiguously. He concludes that their argument is based on weak imaginations and only a penetrating intellect guided by God can navigate such difficult matters. Finally, he quotes Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilimsani criticizing Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for accepting this flawed argument.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
الصفات
الذات
الافتقار
الملازمة
التوقف
الوجوب
الإمكان
واجب الوجود
منع الملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any form of dependency or reliance between two existences necessarily implies contingency and need for an external cause.
Correction: The passage clarifies that if two existences are both necessary and necessarily concomitant (inseparable), their mutual 'dependency' does not entail contingency or need for a cause. Contingency requires the possibility of non-existence, which is absent when both are necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the initial response given by the author to the philosophers' argument?
A) Acceptance of the argument
B) Denial of the entailment (man' al-mulāzama)
C) Denial of the attributes
D) Affirmation that attributes are contingent

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument fails because they equivocate on 'dependency'; causal dependency implies contingency, but mere inseparability does not contradict necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself relying on cherished understandings or familiar terms, are you willing to let the truth of this passage expose where 'dependency' in your own thinking might actually be a hidden assumption that needs correction?
?
In your own intellectual journey, do you ever twist words—like 'dependency'—to defend a position you hold, even when the evidence demands a finer distinction? How will you guard against this sophistry in your own heart?
?
This passage warns against judging by weak imaginations. What comfortable belief about Allah are you holding onto that crumbles when confronted with the precise logic of the scholars? Are you ready to abandon it?

Refuting the Possibility of Divine Attributes
Criticism of a scholar's erroneous view on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that divine attributes are possible in themselves is a dangerous error that compromises divine transcendence and resembles philosophical determinism.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not possible in any sense; they are necessary just as the essence is necessary.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi critiques a scholar who strayed into a serious theological error by applying the category of 'possibility' to the divine attributes. The scholar claimed that the attributes are possible in their own right but rendered necessary by the essence—a statement that echoes the philosophers' explanation of the world's existence. Sanusi shows how this line of reasoning leads to even graver errors about the essence's relation to its attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author builds his criticism step by step.
The passage begins mid-argument; it presumes familiarity with the concept of imkān (possibility).
2
Identify the scholar's claims
Find the two statements attributed to the scholar: one hesitant, one explicit. Understand the progression from doubt to outright assertion.
The scholar first says 'we seek God's guidance,' then boldly declares the attributes possible in themselves.
3
Recognize the comparison with the philosophers
Note how the author likens this view to the philosophic claim about the world (possible in itself, necessary by another). Understand why this comparison is damning from a Sunni perspective.
4
Understand the final condemnation
The author adds that an even worse statement is the claim that the essence is 'receptive' to its attributes and 'agent' of them. Reflect on why this undermines divine simplicity.
This final point highlights the danger of attributing composition or passivity to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُوجِبُ الإمكانَ ، وأنَّ كلَّ مركّب مفتقر إلى جزئهِ ، وجزوه غيرُه ، والمفتقر إلى الغير لا يكون إلا ممكناً ـ وتوهم التركيب باعتبار الصفاتِ ، واستعمل هذه المقدَّماتِ في الاستدلال على إمكان كلٌّ ما سوى الله تعالى .. استشعرَ النقص بصفاتِ اللهِ تعالى ؛ فقالَ مرَّةً : هذا مما نستخيرُ الله تعالى فيهِ - يعني : القول بإمكانها مِنْ حيثُ ذاتها ـ ، وجزم أخرى وصرَّحَ والعياذُ باللهِ بكلمةٍ لم يُسبَق إليها فقال : ممكنة باعتبار ذاتِها ، واجبةٌ بوجوب ذاتِهِ جلَّ وعلا ، وضاها في ذلك قول الفلاسفة : إنَّ العالم ممكن باعتبار ذاتِهِ ، واجب بوجوبِ مقتضيه ، ونعوذُ باللهِ مِنْ زلَّةِ العالِمِ ) انتهى قلتُ : وأشنعُ مِنْ هذا - ونعوذُ باللهِ تعالى - تصريحه بأنَّ الذات قابلة لصفاتها فاعلة لها .
It necessitates possibility, and that every composite is dependent on its part, and its part is other than it, and what is dependent on another is nothing but possible — and he imagined composition with respect to the attributes, and used these premises in deducing the possibility of everything other than God Almighty. He sensed deficiency in the attributes of God Almighty; so he said once: 'This is something we seek God's guidance about' — meaning the statement that they are possible with respect to their essence — and another time he asserted and declared, God forbid, a word that no one had preceded him in, saying: 'They are possible with respect to their essence, and necessary by the necessity of His essence, may He be exalted and glorified.' And in that he resembled the saying of the philosophers: 'The world is possible with respect to its essence, and necessary by the necessity of its determinant.' We seek refuge with God from the slip of the scholar. He said: I say: And more heinous than this — and we seek refuge with God Almighty — is his declaration that the essence is receptive to its attributes and is the agent of them.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage criticizes a scholar (likely a mutakallim) who erroneously applied the concept of possibility to divine attributes. The text begins by summarizing the premises that lead to this error: every composite is dependent on its parts, and what depends on another is possible. This scholar imagined composition in the attributes and used these premises to argue for the possibility of everything other than God. Then he felt a deficiency in God's attributes, so he first said that the attributes are possible in themselves (seeking God's guidance), but later asserted clearly that they are possible in themselves and necessary by the necessity of the essence. The author points out that this resembles the philosophers' claim about the world being possible in itself but necessary by the necessity of its determinant. He then adds that even more heinous is the declaration that the essence is receptive to its attributes and is the agent of them. The author is warning against such statements that compromise divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الإمكان (imkān: possibility)
التركيب (tarkīb: composition)
الصفات (ṣifāt: attributes)
الذات (dhāt: essence)
الفلاسفة (falāsifa: philosophers)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to say that divine attributes are possible in themselves and necessary by the essence.
Correction: The author warns that this statement leads to a similarity with the philosophers' view of the world, compromising God's transcendence, and implies composition and dependency in God.

Checkpoint
What did the scholar assert about the divine attributes that the author criticizes?
A) They are impossible.
B) They are possible in themselves and necessary by the essence.
C) They are identical to the essence.
D) They are eternal and uncreated.

Key Takeaway
Claiming that divine attributes are possible in themselves undermines God's absolute necessity and resembles philosophical determinism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever spoken about Allah's attributes without fully understanding the implications, taking a 'safe' middle ground? What will you do to ensure your words align with the perfection of His essence?
?
When you hear a scholar slip in a seemingly minor point, like 'attributes are possible,' does your heart tremble? How will you guard yourself from the path that leads to resembling the philosophers?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and realizing that a phrase you casually endorsed contradicted His absolute necessity. Will you now commit to studying these matters with humility and seeking His guidance before forming opinions?

Divine Attributes

The Salaf's Balanced Position on Divine Attributes
The author explains the Salaf's prohibition of both otherness and identity for divine attributes and refutes the reductionist view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf forbade both saying the attributes are 'other than' the essence and saying 'they are He,' and how this avoids the extremes of separation and union.

Core Idea
The Salaf steer a middle path: attributes are neither other nor identical to the essence.

In this lesson, we explore a crucial principle of Sunni theology: the precise relationship between Allah's essence and His attributes. The Salaf rejected both extreme formulations—that attributes are 'other than' the essence (which implies separation) and that they are 'identical to' it (which implies union). Instead, they affirmed attributes without comparison or modality, avoiding the philosophical errors of composition and reductionism.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two forbidden extremes
Understand why both 'otherness' and 'identity' are prohibited regarding divine attributes. The same reasoning prevents both statements.
Notice that the prohibition of otherness also implies the prohibition of identity.
2
Examine the root error
See that the underlying problem is fleeing from composition (tarkīb), which led philosophers to deny attributes. The author criticizes a scholar who reduced attributes to mere relations.
The fear of composition is misguided because attributes do not multiply the essence.
3
Grasp the refutation
Learn that multiplicity of attributes does not entail multiplicity in the essence, just as multiple considerations of one thing do not make it many.
This refutes the charge of composition without falling into otherness or identity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع إطلاق الغيريَّةِ في صفاتِ اللهِ تعالى ما يمنعُ أن يُقال : هي هو ومن شنيع مذهبه أيضاً : ردُّهُ الصفاتِ إلى مجرَّدِ نسب وإضافات، وتسميته لها في بعض المواضع مغايرةً للذاتِ، مع ما عُلِمَ مِنْ أَنَّ أئمة السنَّةِ يمنعونَ إطلاق الغيرية في صفاتِهِ تعالى ؛ لما تُؤْذِنُ بهِ مِنْ صحة المفارقة، كما يمنعونَ أنْ يُقالَ : هي هو لما يُؤذِنُ بِهِ مِنْ معنى الاتحادِ، والذي قاده إلى أكثر هذه الآراء الفاسدة بإجماع فرارُهُ من التركيب الذي توهمته الفلاسفة لازماً لثبوتِ الصفاتِ، ولأجل ذلكَ نَفَوْها، هذا معَ أنَّ الشيء لا يتكثَرُ بتكثرِ صفاتِهِ كما لا يتكثّرُ بتكثُرِ اعتباراته .
What prevents the application of otherness (al-ghayriyya) to the attributes of God Almighty also prevents saying 'they are He.' And among the reprehensible aspects of his doctrine is also: his reduction of the attributes to mere relations and addenda, and his calling them in some places 'other than the essence,' despite the known fact that the imams of the Sunnah forbid the application of otherness to His attributes because it implies the possibility of separation, just as they forbid saying 'they are He' because it implies the meaning of union. And what led him to most of these corrupt opinions unanimously is his fleeing from composition (al-tarkīb) which the philosophers imagined to be necessary for the affirmation of the attributes, and for that reason they denied them. This despite the fact that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes, just as it does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its considerations.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the orthodox position on the relationship between God's essence and His attributes. The author states that the same reasoning that prevents saying the attributes are 'other than' the essence also prevents saying 'they are He.' He then criticizes a particular scholar for reducing attributes to mere relations and calling them 'other than the essence.' The Imams of the Sunnah forbid both extremes: 'otherness' because it implies that the attributes can separate from the essence, and 'identity' (saying 'they are He') because it implies union (ittiḥād), which is also inappropriate. The root cause of these errors is fleeing from composition (tarkīb). The philosophers thought that affirming multiple attributes leads to composition in the essence, so they denied the attributes. But the author counters that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes, just as it does not become multiple by the multiplicity of considerations (i.e., different aspects of one thing).

Key Terms
الغيرية (ghayriyya: otherness)
الاتحاد (ittiḥād: union)
التركيب (tarkīb: composition)
النسب والإضافات (nisab wa iḍāfāt: relations and addenda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since we cannot say attributes are 'other than' the essence or 'identical to' it, there is no way to describe their relationship.
Correction: The Sunnah affirms attributes as real and distinct in meaning but not separate or identical to the essence, without comparison (tashbīh) or modality (takyīf).

Checkpoint
Why do the Salaf forbid saying that the attributes are 'other than' the essence?
A) Because it implies that the attributes are created.
B) Because it implies the possibility of separation from the essence.
C) Because it denies the attributes entirely.
D) Because it leads to anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf forbid both saying the attributes are other than the essence and saying they are identical to it, because otherness implies separation and union implies composition, while the truth is that attributes are affirmed without modality or comparison.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of Allah's attributes as either completely separate from Him or as identical to Him? Which tendency do you lean toward, and what specific step can you take today to align your heart with the Salaf's balanced position?
?
The philosophers feared that affirming attributes would make God composite—a being with parts. Have you ever doubted the reality of a divine attribute because it seemed to 'divide' God? How does this lesson's refutation of composition move you to trust the revealed descriptions without rational hesitation?
?
When you reflect on an attribute like Allah's mercy, do you truly feel it is His mercy, not a created entity nor a mere aspect of His essence? How can you train your soul to affirm the attributes as real, while avoiding the extremes of otherness and identity, starting with your next supplication?

Sharf al-Dīn's Argument for Composition in the Essence
Sharf al-Dīn argues composition is necessary for the essence due to distinct attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sharf al-Dīn argues that the distinct attributes imply composition in the divine essence, and how this challenges the philosophers' denial of attributes.

Core Idea
Distinct attributes force composition in the essence itself.

Sharf al-Dīn al-Tilimsānī presents a powerful argument that if you accept the attributes as distinct realities, you must also accept composition in the essence. This passage uses a classification of attributes based on relation (ta'alluq) and effect (ta'thīr) to show that the essence would have multiple aspects, implying composition. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the classification of attributes
Notice the three categories: life (no relation), knowledge (relation but no effect), power and will (relation and effect).
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the attributes are distinct, they require different aspects in the determinant (essence), implying composition.
This is the key point against the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شرفُ الدين : والتركيب في الذاتِ لازم له أيضاً ؛ فإنَّ ماهية كل صفةٍ مِنَ الحياة والعلم والقدرة والإرادة متميزة عن الأخرى في العقل ، فإنَّ منها ما لا يتعلَّقُ وهي الحياةُ ، ومنها ما يتعلَّقُ ولا يؤثر كالعلم، ومنها ما يتعلَّقُ ويؤثر كالقدرةِ والإرادة ، فإذا تمايزت واختلفَتْ اقتضَتْ وجوهاً مختلفة في المقتضي
Sharf al-Dīn said: 'And composition in the essence is also necessary for it; for the quiddity of each attribute among life, knowledge, power, and will is distinct from the other in the intellect. For among them are those that are not related (life), those that are related but do not effect (knowledge), and those that are related and effect (power and will). So when they become distinct and differ, they necessitate different aspects in the determinant.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage quotes Sharf al-Dīn al-Tilimsānī's argument that composition (tarkīb) is also necessary for the divine essence itself if one accepts that attributes are distinct realities. He points out that the quiddity (māhiyya) of each attribute such as life, knowledge, power, and will is mentally distinct from the others. For example, life does not relate (ta'alluq) to anything, knowledge relates but does not effect (ta'thīr), while power and will both relate and effect. Because they are distinct and differ, they require different aspects in the determinant (al-muqtaḍī), i.e., the essence. This suggests that the essence would have multiple aspects, which is a form of composition. This argument is used to show that if the philosophers deny attributes to avoid composition, they must also deny the essence's simplicity, but they do not.

Key Terms

الماهية
التعلق
التأثير
المقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Ashʻarī position leads to composition, but actually Sharf al-Dīn is using this to challenge the philosophers.
Correction: Sharf al-Dīn is not asserting that the essence is composite, but rather that the philosophers' own logic forces them to accept composition if they deny distinct attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sharf al-Dīn, what does the distinction between attributes necessitate?
A) That the essence is simple.
B) That the attributes are identical.
C) Different aspects in the determinant.
D) That attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
Sharf al-Dīn argues that distinct attributes with different relations and effects necessitate composition in the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Consider the attribute of divine knowledge in your life: do you live as though God knows your every action and thought, not as a distant idea but as a real aspect of His essence? What would change today if you truly felt that His knowledge relates to you without effect (as the passage says) but still demands your accountability?

The Philosophers' Denial of Attributes
Sanusi critiques the philosophers' denial of attributes and their deceptive reinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers denied real attributes and reinterpreted them to avoid composition, and why Sanusi argues that this is insufficient for true tawḥīd.

Core Idea
The philosophers' denial of attributes is a deceptive reinterpretation, not true tawḥīd.

In this passage, Sanusi exposes the philosophers' strategy: they rejected real attributes to avoid composition, but continued to use the same terms with empty meanings. He argues that mere negation is insufficient; real attributes must be affirmed. Let's examine his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with the philosophers' motivation and ends mid-sentence, but the key point is clear.
2
Identify the philosophers' motivation and reinterpretation
The philosophers denied attributes because they perceived the problem of composition. They reinterpreted terms like 'knowing' as negations (e.g., not being a body). Find this in the text.
3
Understand Sanusi's critique
Sanusi acknowledges that the philosophers are correct in affirming transcendence (tanzīh), but he insists they must also prove that God is actually knowing, not just negate opposites. This is a crucial point about the positive reality of attributes.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما استشعرَتِ الفلاسفةُ ذلكَ لم يسعهم إلا نفي الصفاتِ ؛ ولبسوا على المسلمين بإطلاقها مع نفي حقائقها ، وفسَّروها بأمورٍ مباينة لماهيَّاتِها ؛ كتفسيرهم كونه عالماً بأنَّه ليس بجسم ولا جسماني، وهم مُساعَدونَ على هذا التنزيه ، ومُطالبون بإثباتِ أَنَّهُ عالم بما

They are supported in this transcendence (tanzīh), but are required to prove that He is knowing of what...
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophers' response to the problem of composition. Once they realized that affirming multiple attributes leads to composition (as argued by Sharf al-Dīn), they chose to deny the attributes entirely. However, they continued to use the same terms (e.g., 'knowing,' 'powerful') but reinterpreted them in ways that contradict their essential meanings. For example, they interpret 'He is knowing' as a negation of being a body or bodily, rather than affirming a real attribute of knowledge. The author notes that they are correct in affirming transcendence (tanzīh), but they are required to also prove that He is actually knowing in a real sense, not just negating imperfections. The passage is cut off, but the argument is that mere negation is insufficient.

Ahl al-Sunna's Position on Divine Attributes
Sanusi states the correct terminology for Allah's attributes and critiques the philosophers' position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the balanced Sunni position on divine attributes, avoiding the extremes of separation and union.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither other than the Essence nor identical to it.

Sanusi presents the correct Sunni terminology for discussing Allah's attributes. He warns against two extremes: treating attributes as separate entities or as identical to the Essence. The root of many errors, he explains, is the fear of composition, which the philosophers misunderstand. This lesson clarifies the Sunni middle path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the two prohibitions and the explanation that follows.
2
Identify the forbidden statements
Find the two things Sanusi says the imams forbid: calling attributes 'other' and saying 'they are He'. Understand why each is problematic.
Forbidding 'otherness' prevents separation; forbidding 'union' prevents incarnation.
3
Trace the root cause
Sanusi explains that the philosophers' error stems from fleeing composition. They thought affirming attributes implies composition, so they denied them.
4
Understand the refutation
A thing does not become multiple by its attributes, just as it does not become multiple by its aspects. This refutes the fear of composition.
Attributes are not parts added to the essence; they are real yet non-multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أئمة السنة يمنعون إطلاق الغيرية في صفاته تعالى؛ لما تؤذن به من صحة المفارقة، كما يمنعون أن يقال: (هي هو) لما يؤذن به من معنى الاتحاد، والذي قاده إلى أكثر هذه الآراء الفاسدة بإجماع فراره من التركيب الذي توهمته الفلاسفة لازماً لثبوت الصفات، ولأجل ذلك نفواها، هذا مع أن الشيء لا يتكثر بتكثر صفاته كما لا يتكثر بتكثر اعتباراته.
The imams of the Sunnah forbid applying [the term] 'otherness' to His attributes—exalted is He—because it implies the correctness of separation; just as they forbid that it be said: 'they are He' because it implies the meaning of union. And that which led them to most of these corrupt opinions, by consensus, was their flight from composition (tarkīb), which the philosophers imagined to be necessary for the affirmation of attributes; and for that reason they denied them. Yet a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes, just as it does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its aspects/considerations.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage defines the correct approach of Ahl al-Sunna to divine attributes. It begins by stating two prohibitions: forbidding the term 'otherness' (al-ghayriyya) for attributes, because it suggests that attributes can be separated from the Essence; and forbidding saying that attributes are identical to the Essence (huma huwa), because it suggests union (ittiḥād), which is characteristic of incarnationist or pantheistic views. The passage then explains the root cause that led to most of the heretical opinions in this matter: the philosophers' flight from the notion of composition (tarkīb). They erroneously thought that affirming attributes necessitates composition, so they denied the attributes altogether. Sanusi refutes this by stating that a thing does not become multiple by the multiplicity of its attributes any more than it becomes multiple by the multiplicity of its conceptual considerations (i'tibārāt).

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that both extremes are forbidden; the Sunni position affirms attributes as real, neither identical nor separate.

Checkpoint

B) Saying they are identical to His essence
C) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that Allah's attributes are neither distinct from His essence nor identical to it, rejecting the extremes of separation and union.

Sharaf al-Dīn's Argument on Composition
Sharaf al-Dīn argues that mental distinction of attributes necessitates composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sharaf al-Dīn's argument that denying real attributes does not escape composition, because the mind necessarily distinguishes between attributes with different properties.

Core Idea
Mental distinction of attributes proves composition in the essence is unavoidable.

Sharaf al-Dīn al-Tilimsānī presents a subtle argument against those who deny that Allah has real attributes. Even if one rejects the existence of attributes as separate realities, the intellect still differentiates between concepts like life, knowledge, power, and will. This differentiation, he argues, forces the denier to admit multiple aspects in the essence, which is itself a form of composition. Let's unfold this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice how Sharaf al-Dīn categorizes the attributes.
Pay attention to the three types he lists.
2
Identify the three categories of attributes
Find the three categories: life (no relation), knowledge (relates but no effect), power/will (relates and effects). Understand what each means.
These categories are based on the attributes' relation to objects.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sharaf al-Dīn concludes that because these attributes are distinct in the mind, the essence must have multiple aspects that necessitate them. This is composition.
The denier of attributes cannot avoid composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شرف الدين: ( والتركيب في الذات لازم له أيضاً؛ فإن ماهية كل صفة من الحياة والعلم والقدرة والإرادة متميزة عن الأخرى في العقل، فإن منها ما لا يتعلق وهي الحياة، ومنها ما يتعلق ولا يؤثر ومنها ما يتعلق ويؤثر كالقدرة والإرادة، فإذا تمايزت واختلفت اقتضت وجوهاً مختلفة في المقتضي لها )
Sharaf al-Dīn said: 'And composition (tarkīb) in the essence is also necessary for him [i.e., for the one who denies attributes]; for the quiddity (māhiyya) of each attribute—life, knowledge, power, will—is distinguished from another in the intellect. Among them is that which does not relate [to objects]—that is life; and among them is that which relates but does not effect—that is knowledge; and among them is that which relates and effects—such as power and will. So when they are distinct and different, they necessitate aspects (wujūh) that are different in the one that necessitates them.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by Sharaf al-Dīn al-Tilimsānī against the philosophers who deny real attributes. The argument is that even if one denies the existence of attributes as distinct realities, the very concepts of the attributes (life, knowledge, power, will) are mentally distinct. In the intellect, they have different properties: life does not relate to objects, knowledge relates but does not effect change, power and will relate and effect change. This differentiation in the intellect forces the one who denies attributes to admit that there are multiple aspects or considerations in the essence that necessitate these distinct attributes. Thus, the denial of attributes does not escape composition; rather, it leads to a composition in the essence itself.

Key Terms

التعلق
التأثير
المقتضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument is merely about mental concepts and not about real composition.
Correction: Sharaf al-Dīn uses mental distinction to show that composition is unavoidable even for the denier—the distinctions in the mind force multiple aspects in the essence.

Checkpoint

B) Because the mind distinguishes between the attributes and their different properties

Key Takeaway
Sharaf al-Dīn shows that even the denier of attributes must admit composition because the mind distinguishes between different types of attributes.

Why Attributes Do Not Multiply the Divine
Sanusi refutes the claim that affirming real attributes leads to multiple eternal beings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming real divine attributes does not necessitate multiple eternal beings, and how Sanusi uses the example of a single atom with multiple attributes to defend the oneness of God.

Core Idea
Attributes do not multiply the subject; multiplicity of attributes does not mean multiplicity of eternal beings.

Sanusi addresses a common objection against the reality of divine attributes: if attributes are real, they would be eternal, leading to multiple eternal beings alongside God. He responds with a clear logical point and an analogy. In this lesson, we will unpack his argument and see how it preserves the unity of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the structure: thesis, objection, answer, evidence, clarification.
2
Identify the objection
Find the opponents' claim: if attributes exist, they would be eternal and thus multiply the eternal beings. This is the key problem Sanusi must solve.
The objection relies on the assumption that real attributes are co-eternal.
3
Understand the answer
Sanusi's answer: the described subject does not become multiple because of its attributes. He gives an example of a single atom (jawhar fard) that has many attributes yet remains one.
The atom analogy is central to grasping the point.
4
Reflect on the consensus
Sanusi clarifies that the consensus 'the Eternal is one' refers to the subject described by divine attributes, not to the attributes themselves. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
The attributes are not additional 'eternal beings'; they are real but inhere in the one God.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to check if you can apply the analogy correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يلزم من ثبوت المعاني تكثر القدماء. قالوا: لو وجدت للزم تكثر القديم بها، والإجماع أن القديم واحد. قلنا: الموصوف لا يتكثر بصفاته؛ بدليل أن الجوهر الفرد يتصف بصفات عديدة وهو واحد، ومعنى الإجماع: أن الموصوف بصفات الألوهية واحد
It does not follow from the existence of meanings (maʿānī) that the eternal beings multiply. They said: If they existed, then the multiplication of the Eternal would be necessary by them, and the consensus is that the Eternal is one. We said: The described (al-mawṣūf) does not become multiple by its attributes; as evidence, a single atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is described by many attributes while it is one. And the meaning of the consensus is that the described with the attributes of divinity is one.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a proof-and-objection-answer passage. Sanusi states the thesis: affirming real attributes (maʿānī) does not necessitate multiple eternal beings. Then he presents the opponents' objection: if the attributes are real, they would be eternal (since they cannot be temporal), leading to multiple eternal beings alongside God, which contradicts the consensus that the Eternal is one. Sanusi's answer: the subject described by attributes does not become multiple because of its attributes. He provides an analogy: the atom (jawhar fard) can have multiple attributes while remaining numerically one. And the consensus about the oneness of the Eternal applies to the subject described by the attributes of divinity, not to the attributes themselves.

Key Terms

القدماء
الجوهر الفرد
الاتصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that real attributes would be additional eternal beings alongside God.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the subject described by attributes remains one, just as an atom with many attributes is one. The consensus only affirms the oneness of the subject, not the attributes.

Checkpoint

A) A composite body
B) A single atom with many attributes
C) A tree with many branches
D) Light and heat

Key Takeaway
Multiplicity of attributes does not imply multiplicity of eternal beings because the subject described remains one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defending Unity Against the Multiplicity Objection
Sanusi answers the second shubha by denying the implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the unity of God against the objection that multiple eternal attributes imply multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of attributes does not breach the unity of the Eternal.

The heretics argue that if God's attributes are eternal realities, then there must be multiple eternal beings, contradicting the consensus that God is one. Sanusi responds by carefully defining what 'multiplicity of the Eternal' means. In this passage, we see how kalam logic distinguishes between the attributes themselves and the subject they describe.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the heretics' claim: if attributes are existing meanings (maʿānī mawjūda) in pre-eternity, then there would be multiple eternal beings (qudamāʾ) alongside God. Notice how they present this as an implication (mulāzama).
The heretics assume that multiple eternal entities necessarily imply a plurality of eternal beings.
2
Follow Sanusi's answer
Sanusi denies the implication (manʿ al-mulāzama). He then clarifies that if by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' you mean composition of the Essence, that does not follow. Multiplicity of attributes does not negate the unity of the described (al-mawṣūf).
3
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on how this argument preserves the absolute unity of God while affirming that His attributes are real and eternal. This is a key distinction in Sunni kalam.
The unity of God (tawḥīd) is not compromised by the multiplicity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه شبهة أخرى للملحدة، قالوا: لو كانت صفات الباري تعالى معاني موجودة .. لكان معه جل وعلا في الأزل قدماء، وهو معنى قولي: (لزم تكثر القديم) والملازمة ظاهرة؛ لأن صفاته جل وعلا يستحيل عليها الحدوث، وأما بطلان التالي: فالإجماع على أن القديم واحد. والجواب: منع الملازمة؛ إن أردتم بـ (تكثر القديم) تركيبه وكثرة أجزائه بسبب وجود الصفات .. فإن كثرة الصفات لا تمنع وحدة الموصوف
This is another shubha (doubt) of the heretics. They said: If the attributes of the Most High were existing meanings (maʿānī mawjūda), then there would be with Him—mighty and majestic—in pre-eternity ancient beings (qudamāʾ), and that is the meaning of my statement: 'the multiplicity of the Eternal (kathrat al-qadīm) would be necessary.' And the implication is apparent, because His attributes—mighty and majestic—are impossible of origination (ḥudūth). As for the falsehood of the consequent: the consensus is that the Eternal is one. And the answer: denial of the implication. If you mean by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' its composition and the multiplicity of its parts due to the existence of the attributes, then indeed the multiplicity of attributes does not prevent the unity of the described (al-mawṣūf).
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This is a second objection-answer passage. The heretics argue that if God's attributes are real entities existing in pre-eternity (azal), then there would be multiple eternal beings (qudamāʾ) alongside God, which violates the consensus that the Eternal (al-qadīm) is one. They insist that the implication is clear because attributes cannot be temporal. Sanusi's answer is to deny the implication (al-mulāzama). He clarifies the meaning of 'multiplicity of the Eternal': if they mean that the Essence would be composed and have parts due to the existence of attributes, then that does not follow, because the multiplicity of attributes does not negate the unity of the subject. The argument hinges on the distinction between the attributes themselves and the Essence; the attributes do not cause a numerical multiplicity of the Essence.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الملازمة
القدماء
الحدوث
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Sanusi denies the consensus that God is one, or that he admits multiple eternal beings.
Correction: Sanusi does not deny the consensus; he reinterprets what 'multiplicity of the Eternal' means. He shows that the existence of attributes does not imply composition or a plurality of eternal beings; the unity of the Essence remains intact.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi answer the second shubha?
A) By denying the consensus
B) By denying the implication (mulāzama)
C) By affirming that attributes are temporal
D) By admitting multiple eternal beings

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of attributes does not necessitate composition or multiple eternal beings; the unity of the Essence remains intact.

Refuting the Claim that Attributes Imply Multiple Eternal Beings
Sanusi refutes an objection about divine attributes implying multiple eternal beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that multiple divine attributes necessitate multiple eternal beings, and why the unity of God is not compromised by His attributes.

Core Idea
An entity is not multiplied by its attributes.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a classic objection against divine attributes: if God has multiple eternal attributes, then there would be multiple eternal beings, contradicting God's oneness. Sanusi dismantles this argument by clarifying what 'multiplicity' means and using the example of the indivisible atom. He exposes the logical fallacies behind the objection, showing that the consensus on God's unity does not negate His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the atheists state their argument: if attributes existed, they would be eternal and thus multiple eternal beings would exist, contradicting the consensus that the eternal is one.
Notice how Sanusi presents the objection clearly before refuting it.
2
Identify Sanusi's first response
Sanusi denies the necessary implication by distinguishing two meanings of 'multiplicity of the eternal'. His first response addresses the idea of composition: attributes do not make the described entity composed or multiple, as seen in the atom example.
The atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is one yet has many attributes like location and indivisibility.
3
Identify Sanusi's second response
If 'multiplicity' means the eternal essence exists in more than one reality, Sanusi accuses the objectors of begging the question. He also clarifies that the consensus on 'the eternal is one' refers to the divine essence described with attributes, not to the concept of pre-eternity itself.
Sanusi points out the equivocation (ishtirak) in the word 'one'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Sanusi notes that this specious argument misled even great thinkers like al-Fakhr al-Razi, emphasizing the importance of sound reasoning in theology.
Consider why Sanusi mentions this—it shows the power of the objection despite its fallacy.

1 لا يلزم من ثبوت المعاني تكثر القدماء ]
قالوا : لو وجدت للزم تكثر القديم بها ، والإجماع أن القديم واحد
•
قلنا : الموصوف لا يتكثر بصفاته ؛ بدليل أن الجوهر الفرد يتصف بصفات عديدة وهو واحد ، ومعنى الإجماع : أن الموصوف بصفات الألوهية واحد

هذه شبهة أخرى للملحدة ، قالوا : لو كانت صفات الباري تعالى معاني موجودة .. لكان معه جل وعلا في الأزل قدماء ، وهو معنى قولي : ( للزم تكثر القديم ( والملازمة ظاهرة ؛ لأن صفاته جل وعلا يستحيل عليها الحدوث، وأما بطلان التالي : فالإجماع على أن القديم واحد .
والجواب : منع الملازمة ؛ إن أردتم بـ ( تكثر القديم ( تركيبه وكثرة أجزائه بسبب وجود الصفات .. فإن كثرة الصفات لا تمنع وحدة الموصوف ولا توجب تركيبه ، ولا يقال فيه بسببها : ( إنه كثير ) لا لغة ولا عرفا ولا عقلا ، ألا ترى أن الجوهر الفرد موصوف بالوحدة وإن اتصف بصفات عديدة ؟! (۱)
وإن أردتم بـ ( تكثر القديم ) وجود معناه في أكثر من حقيقة واحدة . . منعنا الاستثنائية ، ولزمتكم المصادرة عن المطلوب (۲) والإجماع الذي نقلتم على أن القديم واحد يجب أن يكون معناه : أن الأزلي الموصوف بصفات الألوهية جل وعلا واحد لا ثاني له ، لا أن معناه : أن حقيقة القدم لا تثبت إلا لشيء واحد من غير نظر إلى كونه موصوفا أو صفة كما فهمتم
لفظ نعم الواحد قد يطلق على ما قلناه ، وعلى ما ذكرتموه ، فأزيلوا الاشتراك من اللفظ الذي لبستم به ، وقولوا : الأمة مجمعة على أنه لا صفات له ، فلا تجدون حينئذ إلى صحته سبيلا ، وكيف يصح أن ينعقد إجماع على ما قامت البراهين العقلية على خلافه ؟! واعلم : أن هذه الشبهة هي التي غرت الفلاسفة حتى أنكروا جميع الصفات ، وغرت الإمام الفخر حتى قال ما قال ، والله يهدي من يشاء إلى سواء السبيل .
1 It is not necessary that from the establishment of meanings (al-ma'ani) results multiplicity of the eternal (al-qadim). They said: If they existed, it would necessitate multiplicity of the eternal (al-qadim) by them, and the consensus is that the eternal is one. We said: The described is not multiplied by its attributes; evidence is that the atom (al-jawhar al-fard) is described with multiple attributes while being one, and the meaning of consensus is that the described with the attributes of divinity is one. This is another specious argument (shubha) of the atheists. They said: If the attributes of the Exalted Creator were existing meanings, then there would be with Him, may He be glorified and exalted, eternals (qudama') in pre-eternity, and this is the meaning of my statement: 'it would necessitate multiplicity of the eternal.' And the implication (al-mulazama) is apparent because His attributes, may He be glorified and exalted, are impossible to originate. As for the falsity of the consequent: it is the consensus that the eternal is one. The answer: denial of the implication. If by 'multiplicity of the eternal' you mean its composition and multiplicity of its parts due to the existence of attributes, then multiplicity of attributes does not prevent the unity of the described nor necessitate its composition, nor is it said of it because of them 'it is many' neither linguistically, customarily, nor rationally. Do you not see that the atom is described with unity even though it is described with multiple attributes? (1) And if by 'multiplicity of the eternal' you mean the existence of its meaning in more than one reality, then we deny the exceptive syllogism (al-istithna'iyya), and you have committed begging the question (al-musadara) regarding the desired conclusion. (2) And the consensus which you transmitted that the eternal is one must mean: that the eternal described with the attributes of divinity, may He be glorified and exalted, is one with no second to Him, not that its meaning is that the reality of pre-eternity (al-qidam) is only established for one thing without considering whether it is described or an attribute, as you understood. Indeed, the word 'one' may be applied to what we said and to what you mentioned, so remove the equivocation (al-ishtirak) from the word with which you have confused, and say: The community is unanimous that He has no attributes, then you will not find any way to its correctness. And how can a consensus be valid on what rational proofs have demonstrated the opposite of? And know: that this specious argument is what misled the philosophers so that they denied all attributes, and misled al-Imam al-Fakhr until he said what he said, and Allah guides whom He wills to the straight path.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection (shubha) raised by atheists against the existence of divine attributes. The objection argues that if God had multiple attributes existing as meanings (ma'ani), then there would be multiple eternal beings (qudama'), contradicting the consensus that the eternal (qadim) is one. Sanusi first states the objection clearly: the attributes cannot have originated, so they are eternal, leading to multiplicity. The answer begins by denying the necessary implication (mulazama). Sanusi distinguishes two interpretations of 'multiplicity of the eternal'. First, if it means composition or multiplicity of parts due to attributes, he argues that attributes do not compromise the unity of the described entity, using the example of the atom (jawhar fard) which is one yet has many attributes (e.g., location, motion, indivisibility). Second, if it means that the meaning of 'eternal' exists in more than one reality (i.e., more than one eternal being), then Sanusi accuses the objectors of begging the question (musadara) because their premise assumes multiplicity whereas the conclusion is about multiplicity. He then clarifies that the consensus on God's unity means that the divine essence described with attributes is one, not that the concept of pre-eternity (qidam) can only apply to one thing regardless of being essence or attribute. Finally, he points out that the word 'one' is ambiguous (ishtirak) and insists that the objectors must prove that consensus denies attributes altogether, which is impossible since rational proofs support them. Sanusi notes that this very argument misled philosophers and even al-Fakhr al-Razi, showing its deceptive power.

Key Terms
صفات (attributes)
قديم (eternal)
جوهر فرد (atom)
ملازمة (necessary implication)
استثنائية (exceptive syllogism)
مصادرة (begging the question)
اشتراك (equivocation)
شبهة (specious argument)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple attributes necessarily mean multiple independent beings, so affirming attributes compromises God's oneness.
Correction: Attributes do not multiply the essence; they are qualities of a single being. The atom example shows that one entity can have many attributes without being multiple.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi use to show that an entity can have multiple attributes while remaining one?
A) The human soul
B) The atom (al-jawhar al-fard)
C) A body with shape and color
D) The number one

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the objection by showing that attributes do not multiply the essence, using the atom as an analogy, and by clarifying the consensus on God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah because a seemingly logical argument made it appear contradictory? What specific step can you take today to investigate such doubts with sincere scholarship instead of relying on surface reasoning?
?
This passage warns that even great scholars like al-Fakhr al-Razi were misled by a specious argument. Where in your own faith are you most vulnerable to being deceived by intellectual sophistication? What will you do to guard against it?
?
The atom analogy shows that multiplicity of attributes does not imply multiplicity of essence. In your own life, do you sometimes feel fragmented by your many roles and characteristics, forgetting that your identity is fundamentally one? How can this theological truth help you realign your self-perception?

Attributes Do Not Make the Described Many
Sanusi uses the atom analogy to refute the multiplicity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that multiple attributes do not entail multiplicity of the essence, using the indivisible atom as an analogy.

Core Idea
Multiple attributes do not make God many; even an atom has many attributes yet is one.

One common objection against divine attributes is that if God has multiple attributes, He becomes 'many'. Sanusi counters this by pointing to the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is one in essence yet described by many attributes. This lesson unpacks that analogy and shows why the objection fails on linguistic, customary, and rational grounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi presents the claim and then his refutation.
2
Understand the objection
The opponent says: if God has many attributes, then He is 'many'. Identify what 'many' might mean here.
3
Examine the atom analogy
Sanusi uses the indivisible atom (جوهر فرد) as an example. Though it has multiple attributes (location, motion, etc.), it is called 'one'. How does this undermine the objection?
The atom is a physical example; the principle applies to God analogically.
4
Reflect on the implications
If even a physical atom can have many attributes without being many, how much more so for God? This shows the objection is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموصوف ولا تُوجِبُ تركيبه ، ولا يُقالُ فيهِ بسببها : ( إِنَّهُ كَثيرٌ ) لا لغة ولا عرفاً ولا عقلاً ، ألا ترى أن الجوهر الفرد موصوف بالوحدة وإنِ اتَّصف بصفاتٍ عديدةٍ ؟! وإن أردتُم بـ ( تكثرِ القديم ) وجود معناه في أكثرَ مِنْ حقيقةٍ واحدة . . منعنا الاستثنائية ، ولزمتكمُ المصادرة عن المطلوب والإجماع الذي نقلتُم على أنَّ القديم واحدٌ يجب أن يكون معناه : أنَّ الأزلي الموصوف بصفاتِ الأُلوهية جلَّ وعلا واحدٌ لا ثاني له ، لا أَنَّ معناه : أنَّ حقيقة القدم لا تثبتُ إلا لشيء واحدٍ مِنْ غيرِ نظر إلى كونه موصوفاً أو صفة كما فهمتُم لفظ نعم الواحدِ قد يُطلَقُ على ما قلناه ، وعلى ما ذكر تُموه ، فأزيلوا الاشتراكَ مِنَ اللفظِ الذي لبَّسْتُم به ، وقولوا : الأُمَّةٌ مُجمعةٌ على أنَّهُ لا صفاتِ لهُ ، فلا تجدونَ حينئذ إلى صحتِهِ سبيلاً ، وكيف يصح أن ينعقد إجماع على ما قامَتِ البراهين العقلية على خلافِهِ ؟! واعلم : أنَّ هذه الشبهة هي التي غرَّتِ الفلاسفة حتى أنكروا جميعَ الصفاتِ ، وغرَّتِ الإمامَ الفخر حتى قالَ ما قالَ ، والله يهدي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إلى سواء السبيل .
The described does not necessitate its composition, nor is it said due to those attributes: 'It is many' neither linguistically, customarily, nor rationally. Do you not see that the indivisible atom is described by oneness even though it is described by multiple attributes? And if you intend by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' the existence of its meaning in more than one essence, we deny the implication [of the conditional], and you are guilty of begging the question. And the consensus that you cited that the Eternal is one must mean that the Eternal described by the attributes of divinity is one with no second, not that the reality of eternity is only established for one thing without consideration of whether it is described or attribute, as you understood. Indeed, the term 'one' may be applied to what we say and to what you mention. So remove the ambiguity from the term with which you have obfuscated, and say: 'The community is agreed that He has no attributes' - then you will find no way to its correctness. How can a consensus be sound upon what rational proofs have contradicted? Know that this sophism is what deceived the philosophers until they denied all attributes, and deceived al-Imam al-Fakhr until he said what he said. And God guides whom He wills to the straight path.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against divine attributes. The opponent argues that if God has multiple attributes, He would be 'many'. The author counters: a substance like the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) is described as one despite having multiple attributes (e.g., location, motion, color). Thus, multiplicity of attributes does not entail multiplicity of the essence. If the opponent means by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' that the meaning of 'eternal' exists in multiple entities, then the argument is rejected as begging the question (مصادرة) because it assumes what needs to be proven. The author then clarifies the consensus that 'the Eternal is one': it means the Eternal described with divine attributes is one with no second, not that the concept of eternity excludes multiple subjects. The confusion arises from the ambiguous term 'one'. The author challenges the opponent to claim that the Muslim community agrees God has no attributes—such a claim is false and contradicts rational proofs. Finally, the author notes that this sophism misled philosophers and Imam al-Razi.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
كثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having multiple attributes makes God 'many' or composed.
Correction: Attributes do not cause composition or multiplicity, as shown by the indivisible atom's example.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi use to show that multiple attributes do not make something 'many'?
A) The sun
B) The indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد)
C) The human soul
D) Water

Key Takeaway
Multiple attributes do not entail multiplicity or composition; even the indivisible atom, a physical entity, is one despite many attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the many attributes of God—His power, knowledge, mercy—do you ever catch yourself thinking of them as separate 'parts' of Him? How does this lesson challenge you to see them as one undivided reality, and what practical step can you take today to deepen that conviction in your worship?
?
You are a single person with many attributes: anger, kindness, intelligence. Do you ever feel fragmented, like you are many selves? How does the truth that multiplicity does not negate oneness—whether in an atom or in God—help you see your own identity more clearly? What commitment can you make to live more integrated before God?
?
Have you ever casually described God in a way that implies multiplicity, even unintentionally (e.g., 'God has many sides')? What will you change in your speech or internal reflection to honor His absolute oneness as taught in this lesson?

Begging the Question and Clarifying Consensus
Sanusi refutes the opponent by denying the conditional, accusing of begging the question, and clarifying the meaning of consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponent's argument begs the question and how the true meaning of consensus on God's oneness does not exclude attributes.

Core Idea
Consensus does not mean God lacks attributes; the term 'one' is ambiguous.

The opponent further argues that consensus says God is one, implying He cannot have multiple attributes. Sanusi points out this is a misinterpretation: consensus means there is no second God, not that the essence of eternity cannot be shared. He also shows the opponent's conditional argument commits the fallacy of begging the question. This lesson dissects these logical moves and ends with a warning about the harm of this sophism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: conditional denial, accusation of begging the question, then clarification of consensus.
2
Identify the conditional
The opponent says: if by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' you mean existence in more than one essence, then... Sanusi denies the implication.
This is the 'منعنا الاستثنائية' part.
3
Understand begging the question
Sanusi says the opponent is guilty of المصادرة (begging the question). Explain why: they assume that eternity cannot be shared among multiple subjects, which is exactly what needs to be proved.
4
Clarify the consensus
The consensus that 'the Eternal is one' means there is no second God, not that the concept of eternity excludes multiple subjects. The term 'one' is ambiguous.
Sanusi challenges the opponent to claim consensus that God has no attributes, which is false.
5
Reflect on the warning
Sanusi notes that this sophism misled philosophers and even al-Imam al-Fakhr al-Razi. Consider why such arguments are dangerous.

The described does not necessitate its composition, nor is it said due to those attributes: 'It is many' neither linguistically, customarily, nor rationally. Do you not see that the indivisible atom is described by oneness even though it is described by multiple attributes? And if you intend by 'multiplicity of the Eternal' the existence of its meaning in more than one essence, we deny the implication [of the conditional], and you are guilty of begging the question. And the consensus that you cited that the Eternal is one must mean that the Eternal described by the attributes of divinity is one with no second, not that the reality of eternity is only established for one thing without consideration of whether it is described or attribute, as you understood. Indeed, the term 'one' may be applied to what we say and to what you mention. So remove the ambiguity from the term with which you have obfuscated, and say: 'The community is agreed that He has no attributes' - then you will find no way to its correctness. How can a consensus be sound upon what rational proofs have contradicted? Know that this sophism is what deceived the philosophers until they denied all attributes, and deceived al-Imam al-Fakhr until he said what he said. And God guides whom He wills to the straight path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting the Claim That Attributes Imply Multiple Gods
Sanusi refutes the polytheism objection with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the argument that divine attributes would necessitate multiple gods, using two distinct arguments: denying the premise that eternity is a positive attribute and a reductio ad absurdum using the Christian hypostases analogy.

Core Idea
Denying that eternity is a positive attribute dismantles the polytheism objection.

This passage addresses a common objection: if God has eternal attributes, they would share His most specific attribute (eternity) and thus become gods. Sanusi offers two refutations. First, he denies that eternity is a positive attribute, undermining the entire analogy. Second, he turns the argument against the objector, arguing that if Christians are deemed disbelievers for affirming three hypostases, then the objector, who affirms both attributes and the essence, is even more deserving of takfir.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that eternal attributes would be gods because they share in eternity, God's most specific attribute. Identify the logical steps of the argument.
2
Understand the first refutation
Sanusi's first response is to deny that eternity is a positive attribute, let alone a self-attribute or the most specific. This rejects the foundation of the objection.
3
Follow the second objection
The opponents present a refined version: any existing attribute of God must be eternal, and sharing in eternity entails sharing in all divine attributes, making it a god. Read this elaboration.
4
Grasp the Christian hypostases analogy
Sanusi uses a powerful reductio: if Christians are declared disbelievers for affirming three hypostases (life and knowledge), then the objector, who affirms even more attributes, is more deserving of disbelief.
This turns the argument against the opponent.

لا يلزم مِنْ ثبوتِ المعاني تعدد الآلهة قالوا : لو وُجِدَتْ لَلزِمَ تعدُّدُ الآلهة ؛ لمشاركتها له في أخص وصفِهِ وهو القدمُ ، وذلك يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم . قلنا : ممنوع أنَّ القدمَ صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ صفة نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخص هذه شبهة أخرى لهم، وتقريرها : أنَّهم قالوا : لو كانَ لهُ تعالى صفة موجودةٌ لَلزِمَ تعدُّدُ الآلهة ، والتالي معلوم الاستحالة ، فالمقدَّمُ مثله وبيان الملازمة : أنَّ الصفة الموجودة له تعالى لا تكون إلا قديمة ؛ لاستحالة اتصافه جلَّ وعلا بالحوادثِ ، وأخص وصفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا القدم ؛ لانفرادِهِ تعالى بهِ ، والاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم ، فيلزمُ أنْ تكونَ تلك الصفةُ لوجوب قدمها مشاركة للباري تعالى في سائر صفاتِهِ ، فتكون عالمةً قادرةً مريدةً حيَّةً إلى غير ذلكَ مِنْ صفاتِ الإلهِ ، فتكون تلك الصفةُ إلها ، فقد لزمَ مِنْ وجودِ الصفة تعدُّدُ الإله . وأيضاً : إذا كُفِّرَتِ النصارى بإثباتهم الأقانيم الثلاثةَ ؛ والحياةُ والعلم . . فأنتم الذينَ أثبتُم ذلك وزيادةً أولى بالتكفير
The existence of attributes does not necessitate multiple gods. They said: If they existed, it would necessitate multiple gods, because they share with Him in His most specific attribute, which is eternity, and that necessitates sharing in the more general. We say: It is denied that eternity is a positive attribute, let alone that it is a self-attribute, let alone that it is the most specific. This is another sophism of theirs, and its explanation: They said: If He, exalted, had an existing attribute, it would necessitate multiple gods, and the consequent is known to be impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. And the demonstration of the implication: The existing attribute of Him, exalted, can only be eternal due to the impossibility of His being described by originated things. And the most specific attribute of the Creator, exalted, is eternity because He alone is distinguished by it. Sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general, so it would follow that that attribute, due to the necessity of its eternity, shares with the Creator in all His other attributes, so it would be knowing, powerful, willing, living, and so on from the attributes of divinity, so that attribute would be a god. Thus the existence of an attribute necessitates multiplicity of gods. And also: If the Christians are declared disbelievers for affirming three hypostases, and life and knowledge, then you who have affirmed that and more are more deserving of declaring disbelief.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that divine attributes would be additional gods. The objection: if God has an attribute (e.g., knowledge) that is eternal, then that attribute shares with God the most specific attribute (eternity), and sharing in the most specific entails sharing in all general attributes (e.g., power, will, life). Hence the attribute would be a god, leading to polytheism. The author's refutation: first, it is denied (ممنوع) that eternity is a positive (ثبوتية) attribute, let alone a self-attribute (نفسية), let alone the most specific. This undermines the entire analogy. The author then presents a second refutation: if Christians are deemed disbelievers for affirming three hypostases (life and knowledge), then the opponent, by affirming both attributes and the essence, is even more deserving of takfir. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
قدم
صفة ثبوتية
صفة نفسية
أخص وصف
مشاركة
تعدّد الآلهة
أقانيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal attributes of God must be separate gods because they share in the attribute of eternity.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the premise that eternity is a positive attribute (صفة ثبوتية) is false; thus the argument collapses. Additionally, even if one accepts the premise, the reductio argument exposes the inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first response to the claim that attributes share in the most specific attribute (eternity)?
A) He agrees and uses it to prove multiplicity
B) He denies that eternity is a positive attribute
C) He says the attribute is not eternal
D) He says sharing in the most specific does not imply sharing in the general

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the polytheism objection by denying that eternity is a positive attribute and by turning the argument against the objector using the Christian hypostases analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a complex theological objection, does your first reaction become defensive or do you patiently examine the premises? The opponents in this passage used a seemingly logical argument, but it was based on false assumptions. How can you train yourself to scrutinize your own assumptions about Allah's attributes? This week, identify one attribute you may have misunderstood and seek correction from sound sources.
?
The analogy with Christian hypostases is powerful: they are condemned for affirming three, yet you affirm more. If you were to be held to the same standard, would your beliefs about Allah's attributes be free from any trace of association? Examine your heart: is there any subtle shirk in your understanding of His attributes? Resolve to purify your tawheed today.
?
How often do you rely on your own reasoning alone to understand God, without submitting to the guidance of the Qur'an and Sunnah? In what areas of your belief might you be inadvertently making assumptions that lead to a distorted view of tawheed? Commit to seeking knowledge from scholars and the revealed texts rather than mere logic.
