Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Initial Objection and Answer
Objection and initial answer regarding qidam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic objection that eternal attributes would share God's most specific attribute (qidam) and the initial answer that qidam is not a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Qidam is a negation, not a positive attribute, so it cannot be the most specific.

The objector argues that if God has attributes, they must be eternal and thus share in His most specific attribute, qidam. This would make them gods. The author begins his reply by directly challenging whether qidam is even a positive attribute, let alone the most specific. Let's examine this initial exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully: the objector's claim and the author's brief response. Note the use of 'mummaniʿ' (it is prevented).
2
Understand 'positive attribute'
Focus on the terms صفة ثبوتية (positive attribute) and صفة نفسية (self-attribute). These are technical distinctions in kalam.
A positive attribute is one that adds a meaning to the essence; a self-attribute is one that is identical with the essence.
3
Recognize qidam as a negation
The author states that qidam cannot even be a positive attribute. This hints at the later argument that qidam is a negation.
This is the key insight: qidam is not a property that exists, but a lack of beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يلزم مِنْ ثبوتِ المعاني تعدد الآلهة ]
قالوا : لو وُجِدَتْ لَلزِمَ تعدُّدُ الآلهة ؛ لمشاركتها له في أخص وصفِهِ وهو القدمُ ، وذلك يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم .
قلنا : ممنوع أنَّ القدمَ صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ صفةً نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخص .
هذه شبهة أخرى لهم، وتقريرها : أنَّهم قالوا : لو كانَ لهُ تعالى صفة موجودةٌ لَلزِمَ تعدُّدُ الآلهة ، والتالي معلوم الاستحالة ، فالمقدَّمُ مثله .
وبيان الملازمة : أنَّ الصفة الموجودة له تعالى لا تكون إلا قديمة ؛ لاستحالة اتصافه جلَّ وعلا بالحوادثِ ، وأخص وصفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا القدم ؛ لانفرادِهِ تعالى بهِ ، والاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم ، فيلزمُ أنْ تكونَ تلك الصفةُ لوجوب قدمها مشاركة للباري تعالى في سائر صفاتِهِ ، فتكون عالمةً قادرةً مريدةً حيَّةً إلى غير ذلكَ مِنْ صفاتِ الإلهِ ، فتكون تلك الصفةُ إلها ، فقد لزمَ مِنْ وجودِ الصفة تعدُّدُ الإله .
وهي
وأيضاً : إذا كُفِّرَتِ النصارى بإثباتهم الأقانيم الثلاثةَ ؛ والحياةُ والعلم . . فأنتم الذينَ أثبتُم ذلك وزيادةً أولى بالتكفير
والجواب : منعُ الملازمةِ ؛ فإِنَّ القدم لا يكونُ أخص ، وكيف وهو سلب ؟! لأنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن نفي سبقِ العَدمِ ، ونفي هذه الإضافة سلبٌ لا محالة ، والبارى جلَّ وعلا موجود ، وأخص وصْفِ الموجودِ لا يكونُ عدماً ؛ لأنَّ الأخص مُقوّم للشيء ، والشيء لا يتقوم بنقيضه الذي هو العدم .
[ الوصف الأخص ]
وبالجملة : فالأخص لا يكون إلا وصفاً ثابتاً ذاتياً ، وليسَ أيضاً كلُّ ذاتي أخص ؛ فإنَّ الحيوانية ذاتية للإنسان ، وليسَتْ أخص وصفٍ ، بل الأخصُّ هو الذاتي الذي به تقوَّمَتِ الماهية وامتازت غيرها ؛ كالنفس الناطقة للإنسان مثلاً
فإذا كانَ الوصفُ سلباً فبينَهُ وبينَ الأخص مراحل ، وإلى هذا أشرتُ بقولي في العقيدة : ( ممنوع أنَّ القدم صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكون صفةً نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخصَّ ) أي : لم يثبت للقدم أوَّلُ مراتب الأخص ؛ وهو الثبوتُ ، فكيفَ يثبتُ لهُ أعلاها ؛ وهو الأخصية ؟!
It is not necessary from the affirmation of attributes (al-maʿānī) the multiplicity of gods. They said: If they existed, multiplicity of gods would be necessary, because they share with Him in His most specific attribute (akhaṣṣ waṣf), which is pre-eternity (al-qidam), and that necessitates sharing in the more general. We said: It is prevented that pre-eternity is a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya), let alone that it be a self-attribute (ṣifa nafsiyya), let alone that it be the most specific. This is another doubt of theirs, and its exposition: that they said: If He, exalted, had an existing attribute, multiplicity of gods would be necessary, and the consequent is known to be impossible, so the antecedent is likewise. And the demonstration of the entailment: that the existing attribute of Him, exalted, cannot but be eternal, because of the impossibility of His, majestic and exalted, being described by originated events (al-ḥawādith), and the most specific attribute of the Creator, majestic and exalted, is pre-eternity, because He alone is characterized by it, and sharing in the most specific necessitates sharing in the more general, so it follows that that attribute, due to the necessity of its pre-eternity, shares with the Creator, exalted, in all His attributes, so it would be knowing, powerful, willing, living, and so on from the attributes of God (al-ilāh), so that attribute would be a god, so from the existence of the attribute multiplicity of god is necessitated. And also: If the Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three hypostases (aqānīm) – life and knowledge… then you who affirmed that and more are more deserving of takfīr. And the answer: prevention of the entailment; for pre-eternity is not the most specific, and how could it be, while it is a negation (salb)?! Because it is an expression of the negation of priority of nonexistence, and the negation of this addition is a negation without doubt, and the Creator, majestic and exalted, is existent, and the most specific attribute of the existent cannot be nonexistence; because the most specific is constitutive (muqawwim) of the thing, and a thing is not constituted by its opposite which is nonexistence. [ The most specific attribute ] And in summary: the most specific cannot be except a positively established essential attribute (waṣf thābit dhātī), and not every essential attribute is most specific; for animality is essential to man but is not the most specific attribute, rather the most specific is the essential attribute by which the essence (al-māhiyya) is constituted and distinguished from others; like the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) for man for example. So if the attribute is a negation, then between it and the most specific are stages, and this is what I indicated in my saying in al-ʿAqīda: 'It is prevented that pre-eternity is a positive attribute, let alone that it be a self-attribute, let alone that it be the most specific' – meaning: the first rank of the most specific, which is [positive] affirmation (al-thubūt), has not been established for pre-eternity, so how can its highest rank, which is being the most specific, be established for it?!
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the Ashʿarī doctrine of attributes. The objector argues: if God has attributes (like knowledge, power), those attributes would be eternal (qadīma), and thus share with God in His most specific attribute, qidam (pre-eternity). Since sharing in the most specific entails sharing in all more general attributes, each attribute would possess all divine attributes, thus each would be a god, leading to polytheism. The author first gives a brief reply: 'mummaniʿ' (it is prevented) that qidam is a positive attribute (thubūtiyya), let alone a self-attribute (nafsiyya), let alone being the most specific. Then the objector is presented again with a more detailed restatement and an additional argument comparing Muslims to Christians: Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three hypostases (Life, Knowledge), while Muslims affirm many more, so they are more deserving of takfīr. The author's main answer ('al-jawāb') is to deny the entailment (manʿ al-mulāzama). The reason: qidam is not a positive attribute but a negation (salb); it means 'negation of prior nonexistence'. A negation cannot be the most specific attribute of an existent, because the most specific attribute must be constitutive (muqawwim) of the thing. A thing cannot be constituted by its own negation. Then the author explains what an 'most specific attribute' truly is: it must be a positively established essential attribute (thābit dhātī) by which the essence is constituted and distinguished, like the rational soul for humans. Since qidam is a negation, it is far removed from being the most specific. The author concludes by referring to his earlier statement in al-ʿAqīda: qidam fails even the first rank of the most specific (positive existence), so it cannot attain the highest rank (being the most specific).

Key Terms
قدم
صفة ثبوتية
صفة نفسية
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qidam is a positive attribute because it is unique to God.
Correction: The author points out that qidam is a negation (salb) meaning 'absence of beginning', not a positive existence. Therefore it cannot be a constitutive attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the author's initial response to the objection?
A) He accepts that attributes lead to multiple gods.
B) He denies that qidam is a positive attribute.
C) He argues that attributes are not eternal.
D) He says that sharing qidam is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The initial answer denies that qidam is a positive attribute, which undercuts the objection that attributes share God's most specific attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'pre-eternal', do you imagine it as a positive property like 'old' but infinitely extended? How does knowing that qidam is merely a negation of beginning change your mental image? Will you now consciously avoid attributing a positive 'thing' to God when you say He is qadīm?
?
The objection's power lies in assuming that sharing a most specific attribute leads to equal divinity. Have you ever felt that someone sharing a special quality with you diminishes your uniqueness? How does this lesson's denial of qidam as a positive attribute free you from competitive thinking about God? Commit to seeing God's uniqueness as beyond all comparisons, even negative ones.
?
If the most specific attribute is not positive, then what does it really mean to say God is 'unique'? Are you content to define uniqueness as a negation (no beginning) rather than a positive, graspable quality? Pledge to deepen your tawhid by embracing negative theology when appropriate.

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Detailed Refutation: Qidam as Negation
Detailed refutation and definition of 'most specific attribute'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam cannot be the most specific attribute because it is a negation, and what truly constitutes an 'most specific attribute'.

Core Idea
A negation cannot be the most specific attribute of an existent.

The objector restates his argument more fully and adds a comparison with Christian trinitarianism. The author's main answer is a 'denial of entailment' (manʿ al-mulāzama) by showing that qidam is a negation, not a positive attribute. He then explains what a genuine 'most specific attribute' must be: a positive, essential, constitutive attribute. Let's unpack this detailed refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed objection
Read from 'هذه شبهة أخرى لهم' to 'أولى بالتكفير'. Note how the objection is expanded and the Christian analogy is introduced.
The Christian comparison is a rhetorical device to emphasize the supposed absurdity of multiple attributes.
2
Analyze the main answer
Read from 'والجواب' to 'مراحل'. The author denies the entailment by arguing that qidam is a negation (salb), not a positive attribute, so it cannot be the most specific.
Key statement: 'وكيف وهو سلب؟!' (How can it be, while it is a negation?!)
3
Understand the definition of 'most specific'
Read from 'وبالجملة' to the end. The author clarifies that the most specific attribute must be a positively established essential attribute (thābit dhātī) that constitutes the essence and distinguishes it, like the rational soul for humans.
Qidam fails this definition because it is a negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Because it is an expression of the negation of priority of nonexistence, and the negation of this addition is a negation without doubt, and the Creator, majestic and exalted, is existent, and the most specific attribute of the existent cannot be nonexistence; because the most specific is constitutive (muqawwim) of the thing, and a thing is not constituted by its opposite which is nonexistence. [ The most specific attribute ] And in summary: the most specific cannot be except a positively established essential attribute (waṣf thābit dhātī), and not every essential attribute is most specific; for animality is essential to man but is not the most specific attribute, rather the most specific is the essential attribute by which the essence (al-māhiyya) is constituted and distinguished from others; like the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) for man for example. So if the attribute is a negation, then between it and the most specific are stages, and this is what I indicated in my saying in al-ʿAqīda: 'It is prevented that pre-eternity is a positive attribute, let alone that it be a self-attribute, let alone that it be the most specific' – meaning: the first rank of the most specific, which is [positive] affirmation (al-thubūt), has not been established for pre-eternity, so how can its highest rank, which is being the most specific, be established for it?!
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the Ashʿarī doctrine of attributes. The objector argues: if God has attributes (like knowledge, power), those attributes would be eternal (qadīma), and thus share with God in His most specific attribute, qidam (pre-eternity). Since sharing in the most specific entails sharing in all more general attributes, each attribute would possess all divine attributes, thus each would be a god, leading to polytheism. The author first gives a brief reply: 'mummaniʿ' (it is prevented) that qidam is a positive attribute (thubūtiyya), let alone a self-attribute (nafsiyya), let alone being the most specific. Then the objector is presented again with a more detailed restatement and an additional argument comparing Muslims to Christians: Christians were declared disbelievers for affirming three hypostases (Life, Knowledge), while Muslims affirm many more, so they are more deserving of takfīr. The author's main answer ('al-jawāb') is to deny the entailment (manʿ al-mulāzama). The reason: qidam is not a positive attribute but a negation (salb); it means 'negation of prior nonexistence'. A negation cannot be the most specific attribute of an existent, because the most specific attribute must be constitutive (muqawwim) of the thing. A thing cannot be constituted by its own negation. Then the author explains what an 'most specific attribute' truly is: it must be a positively established essential attribute (thābit dhātī) by which the essence is constituted and distinguished, like the rational soul for humans. Since qidam is a negation, it is far removed from being the most specific. The author concludes by referring to his earlier statement in al-ʿAqīda: qidam fails even the first rank of the most specific (positive existence), so it cannot attain the highest rank (being the most specific).

Key Terms
سلب
ذاتي
أخص وصف
ماهم
ثبوت
نفس ناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that qidam is a positive attribute that defines God's essence.
Correction: The author shows that qidam is a negation (salb) and therefore cannot be a constitutive attribute. The most specific attribute must be positive and essential.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that qidam cannot be the most specific attribute?
A) Because qidam is shared by other beings.
B) Because qidam is a negation, and the most specific must be a positive constitutive attribute.
C) Because qidam is not an attribute at all.
D) Because the most specific attribute must be unique to God.

Key Takeaway
Qidam is a negation, not a positive attribute, so it cannot be the most specific attribute; the most specific must be a positive essential constitutive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If qidam is merely a negation, does the eternity of God feel less real to you? How does this affect your worship—do you worship a God whose eternity is no more than the absence of beginning, or do you need a positive attribute to hold onto? Write down one practical change you will make in your remembrance of God's eternity.
?
The objector accuses Muslims of falling into the same error as Christians regarding divine attributes. Have you ever felt uncomfortable with theological complexities, fearing they might compromise tawhid? How does this lesson's precision help you trust that the kalām tradition safeguards God's oneness without stripping Him of His attributes? Commit to studying one more aspect of divine attributes with the same rigor.
?
The author insists that the most specific attribute must be positively constitutive. Think about how you describe God to yourself. Do you rely on negations (like 'not created') more than affirmations (like 'alive, knowing, powerful')? Are you comfortable with the fact that your understanding of God's essence is built on positive attributes, not just negations? This week, in your personal supplication, focus on addressing God by one of His positive names and reflect on its meaning.

Why Qidam Cannot Be the Most Specific Attribute
Sanusi argues that qidam is a negative attribute and cannot be the most specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qidam (pre-eternity) cannot be the most specific attribute of God, based on Sanusi's argument that it is a negative attribute and the most specific must be positive and constitutive.

Core Idea
Qidam is a negation, so it cannot be the most specific attribute.

Sanusi continues his discussion of divine attributes by addressing a potential objection. An opponent might claim that qidam (pre-eternity) is the most specific attribute (al-akhas) of God. Sanusi refutes this by showing that qidam is a negative attribute—it denies prior non-existence—and therefore cannot be constitutive of existence. He then explains what truly qualifies as the most specific attribute, using the example of the rational soul for humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi begins by denying the entailment and then provides reasons.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify Sanusi's main claim
Sanusi's main claim is that qidam cannot be the most specific attribute. Find the key sentence where he states this and his first reason (qidam is a negation).
He says: 'فإن القدم لا يكون أخص، وكيف وهو سلب؟'
3
Understand the definition of al-akhas
Sanusi defines the most specific attribute as a positive essential attribute that constitutes the quiddity and distinguishes it. Note the example of the rational soul for humans.
Contrast with animality, which is essential but not most specific.
4
Apply the definition to qidam
Since qidam is a negation (salb), it cannot be a positive attribute, let alone constitutive. Therefore, it fails to meet the criteria for al-akhas.
Sanusi emphasizes that qidam is not even a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya).
5
Reflect on the concluding verse
Read the final part where Sanusi cites his own 'Aqida. Notice the logical progression: from denying qidam as thubutiyya to denying it as nafsiyya to denying it as akhas.
This summarizes the argument in a concise form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : منعُ الملازمةِ ; فإِنَّ القدم لا يكونُ أخص ، وكيف وهو سلب ؟! لأنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن نفي سبقِ العَدمِ ، ونفي هذه الإضافة سلبٌ لا محالة ، والبارى جلَّ وعلا موجود ، وأخص وصْفِ الموجودِ لا يكونُ عدماً ; لأنَّ الأخص مُقوّم للشيء ، والشيء لا يتقوم بنقيضه الذي هو العدم . [ الوصف الأخص ] وبالجملة : فالأخص لا يكون إلا وصفاً ثابتاً ذاتياً ، وليسَ أيضاً كلُّ ذاتي أخص ; فإنَّ الحيوانية ذاتية للإنسان ، وليسَتْ أخص وصفٍ ، بل الأخصُّ هو الذاتي الذي به تقوَّمَتِ الماهية وامتازت غيرها ; كالنفس الناطقة للإنسان مثلاً فإذا كانَ الوصفُ سلباً فبينَهُ وبينَ الأخص مراحل ، وإلى هذا أشرتُ بقولي في العقيدة : ( ممنوع أنَّ القدم صفةٌ ثبوتية ، فضلاً أنْ يكون صفةً نفسيَّةً ، فضلاً أنْ يكونَ أخصَّ ) أي : لم يثبت للقدم أوَّلُ مراتب الأخص ; وهو الثبوتُ ، فكيفَ يثبتُ لهُ أعلاها ; وهو الأخصية ؟!
The answer: the entailment is denied. For pre-eternity (qidam) cannot be the most specific attribute (akhas) — how can it, when it is a negation (salb)? Because it is an expression of the negation of priority of non-existence, and the negation of this relation is certainly a negation. The Exalted Creator exists, and the most specific description of the existent cannot be non-existence, because the most specific is constitutive of the thing, and the thing is not constituted by its opposite, which is non-existence. [The Most Specific Attribute] In summary: the most specific (al-akhas) can only be a positive, essential attribute. And not every essential attribute is most specific — for animality is essential to man but is not the most specific attribute. Rather, the most specific is the essential attribute by which the quiddity is constituted and distinguished from others, like the rational soul for man, for example. So if the attribute is a negation, between it and the most specific there are stages. And this is what I alluded to in my saying in the 'Aqida: 'It is denied that pre-eternity is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya), let alone that it be a selfal attribute (sifa nafsiyya), let alone that it be the most specific.' That is: the first rank of the most specific — which is positiveness (thubut) — is not established for pre-eternity, so how can its highest rank — which is being the most specific (akhasiyya) — be established for it?
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the position that qidam (pre-eternity) cannot be the most specific attribute (al-akhas) of God. The author begins by denying the entailment presupposed by the opponent: 'the entailment is denied' — that is, the claim that qidam could be the most specific attribute is rejected. The reason is that qidam is a negative attribute (salb), defined as the negation of priority of non-existence (nafy sabq al-‘adam). Since it is a negation, it cannot be constitutive of existence; rather, the most specific attribute must be positive and constitutive. The author then explains that the most specific attribute (al-akhas) is an essential attribute that constitutes the quiddity and distinguishes it from others, like the rational soul for humans. He then applies this to qidam: if the attribute is a negation, it is far from being the most specific. Finally, he cites his own verse from the 'Aqida: 'Mumtani‘ anna al-qidama sifa thubutiyya...' to emphasize that qidam is not even a positive attribute, so it cannot be a selfal attribute or the most specific.

Key Terms

أخص (akhas)
سلب (salb)
صفة ثبوتية (sifa thubutiyya)
صفة نفسية (sifa nafsiyya)
مقوم (muqawwim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qidam is a positive attribute like other positive attributes of God.
Correction: Qidam is defined as the negation of prior non-existence, making it a negative attribute (salb). It does not denote a positive quality but rather the absence of beginning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author deny that qidam can be the most specific attribute (al-akhas)?
A) Because qidam is a positive attribute.
B) Because qidam is a negative attribute (salb), and the most specific must be positive and constitutive.
C) Because qidam is not essential.
D) Because qidam is not unique to God.

Key Takeaway
Qidam cannot be the most specific attribute of God because it is a negation, while the most specific must be a positive, constitutive attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's pre-eternity, do you find yourself focusing more on what He is not (not subject to time) rather than on positive descriptions like His existence or power? How might this lesson correct an imbalance in your understanding of God's attributes? What practical step can you take today to affirm a positive attribute of God in your prayer or reflection?
?
The author argues that the most specific attribute must be constitutive and positive. If you were to identify the single most important attribute that defines your relationship with God, would it be a negative description (like 'He has no beginning') or a positive one (like 'He is merciful')? How does that choice shape your worship?
?
This lesson challenges the assumption that any unique attribute of God can be the most defining. Are there any of your own assumptions about God that you have uncritically accepted? How can you cultivate a habit of scrutinizing your beliefs about God to ensure they align with precise theological reasoning?

The Mu'tazili Argument and Its Dialectical Refutation
Sanusi presents the Mu'tazili argument and gives a dialectical refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili proof that commonality in the specific forces commonality in the general, and how a dialectical answer turns the argument back on them.

Core Idea
A shared attribute doesn't force total likeness; dialectics can flip the accusation.

Sanusi begins by explaining a key Mu'tazili argument: if God had knowledge, it would relate to specific objects like ours, so both must share the same properties—leading to the impossible conclusion that both are either eternal or originated. He then provides a dialectical counter, showing that the same logic applies to the Mu'tazila's own affirmation of God's omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument and the dialectical response.
Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazili proof, then the answer.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili argument
Find the premises: God has knowledge, it relates to specific objects as ours does, commonality in the specific (relation to a specific known) forces commonality in the general (knowledge itself), leading to either both pre-eternal or both temporal.
The conclusion 'both impossible' is the crux.
3
Understand the dialectical answer
See how Sanusi says the same argument applies to the Mu'tazila because they affirm God's omniscience (ʿālimiyya) which also relates to known objects, forcing them into the same dilemma.
This is a reductio ad absurdum, not a final refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان متى يكون الاشتراك في الأخص موجباً للاشتراك في الأعم] ومن معنى شبهة المعتزلة السابقة - وهي إلزام الاشتراك في الأعم لأجل الاشتراك في الأخصّ الذي هو القدم (۲) : احتجاجهم بأنَّهُ لو كانَ اللهِ تعالى علمٌ لكانَ علمُهُ يتعلَّق بعين ما يتعلَّق بهِ علمُنا ، وأخص وصْفِ علمنا تعلّقه بالمعلومِ المعيَّنِ ، والاشتراك في الأخص يُوجِبُ الاشتراك في الأعم ، فيجب إذاً مماثلة علمهِ تعالى لعلمنا ، فيلزمُ إِمَّا قدمهما ، وإما حدوثهما ، وكلاهما محال . والجواب : أنَّ هذا مشتركُ الإلزام ؛ لأنَّه يلزمهم مثله فيما أثبتوا الله تعالى مِنَ العالِميَّةِ ؛ فإنَّ عالِميَّتَهُ تعالى إذا تعلَّقَتْ بالمعلومِ وتعلَّقتْ عالِميتُنا به.. لزمهم عينُ ما ألزمونا ، وهذا جواب جدلي . والجواب الحقُّ : أنَّ الاشتراك في الأخصّ إِنَّما يستلزم الاشتراك في الأعم الذاتي ، والحدوث والقدم ليسا بذاتيين ؛ لعدم توقف فهم الماهية عليهما ، فإنَّا نتعقَّلُ العلم معَ الذهول عن كونه قديماً أو حادثاً ، ثم نقيم الدليل بعد ثبوتِهِ على أنَّهُ قديم أو حادث
Explanation of when commonality in the more specific necessitates commonality in the more general. And from the meaning of the previous Mu'tazilite sophism — which is the imposition of commonality in the more general due to commonality in the more specific, which is pre-eternity (2): their argument that if Allah had knowledge, then His knowledge would relate to the very same thing that our knowledge relates to, and the most specific description of our knowledge is its relation to a specific known object. Commonality in the more specific necessitates commonality in the more general, so therefore the likeness of His knowledge to our knowledge is necessary, and hence either both are pre-eternal or both are temporally originated, and both are impossible. The answer: This is a shared imposition, because the same applies to them regarding what they affirm for Allah the Exalted of omniscience; for if His omniscience relates to the known and our omniscience relates to it, then the same thing we charged them with applies to them. And this is a dialectical answer. The true answer: Commonality in the more specific necessitates commonality only in essential general attributes, and temporal origination and pre-eternity are not essential, because understanding the essence does not depend on them; for we conceive knowledge while being heedless of whether it is pre-eternal or temporally originated, then we establish the proof after its existence that it is pre-eternal or temporally originated.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazili argument (shubha). It begins by stating the topic: under what conditions does commonality in the more specific (al-akhass) necessitate commonality in the more general (al-aʿamm)? The Mu'tazili argument is then presented: if God had knowledge, it would relate to the same specific object as human knowledge, and since the most specific description of human knowledge is its relation to a specific known object, commonality in this specific attribute forces commonality in the general category of knowledge, leading to the impossible conclusion that either both are pre-eternal or both are temporal. Two answers are given. The first is a dialectical (jadali) answer: the same argument can be turned against the Mu'tazila because they affirm God's omniscience, which would also relate to the same objects as human omniscience, thus forcing them into the same dilemma. The second is the true answer (al-jawab al-haqq): commonality in the specific only entails commonality in essential (dhati) general attributes, and pre-eternity (qidam) and temporal origination (huduth) are not essential attributes of knowledge because the essence of knowledge can be conceived without considering whether it is pre-eternal or originated; these attributes are established by proof after the essence is known. The passage follows the order: statement of topic, presentation of the argument, dialectical answer, true answer.

Key Terms
شبهة المعتزلة
الأخص
الأعم
القدم
الحدوث
الجواب الجدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commonality in any specific attribute (like relation to a known object) forces commonality in all general attributes (like being knowledge).
Correction: The dialectical answer shows the argument is self-defeating, but the true answer (next lesson) clarifies that only essential general attributes are entailed, and pre-eternity/temporality are not essential.

Checkpoint
What does the Mu'tazili argument claim commonality in the specific (al-akhass) necessitates?
A) Commonality in the general (al-aʿamm)
B) Essential attributes only
C) Identity of the object
D) Both pre-eternity and temporal origination

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili argument uses the principle that commonality in specific attributes forces commonality in general attributes; Sanusi counters dialectically by turning the same logic against them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain of a logical argument about God, do you ever pause to consider that your reasoning might be flawed? How does the Mu'tazili argument's elegant logic feel when it is turned against its proponents—does that shake your confidence in pure reason to capture divine realities? Commit to re-examining one belief you hold about God that you base solely on logical inference, and ask yourself: Could the same logic be used to argue the opposite?
?
The dialectical response here is powerful but temporary—it wins the debate without settling the truth. Are you more interested in winning theological arguments or in humbly seeking the truth, even if it leaves you with unanswered questions? Will you promise to study the deeper Ash'ari refutation (like the distinction between essential and relational attributes) rather than being satisfied with a clever counter-argument?
?
You share with the Mu'tazila the desire to defend God's transcendence. But could your own theology have hidden assumptions—like the principle that shared specifics force shared generals—that you have never examined? Take one attribute of God you affirm and trace the logical steps you use to support it. Are any of those steps borrowed from human experience in a way that might not apply to the Creator?

The True Refutation: Essential vs. Non-Essential Attributes
Sanusi gives the true refutation distinguishing essential from non-essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true refutation of the Mu'tazili argument: commonality in specific attributes only entails commonality in essential general attributes, and pre-eternity/temporal origination are not essential.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are intrinsic; pre-eternity is not part of the essence of knowledge.

After the dialectical counter, Sanusi delivers the definitive answer: the Mu'tazili principle only works for essential (dhati) attributes. Pre-eternity and temporal origination are not essential to the concept of knowledge, because we can conceive knowledge without considering its age. This distinction dismantles the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the true answer passage
Read the Arabic for the true answer and its translation. Focus on the key distinction between essential and non-essential attributes.
The word 'dhati' is crucial.
2
Understand the essential vs. non-essential distinction
Sanusi argues that qidam (pre-eternity) and huduth (temporal origination) are not essential because we can understand the essence (mahiyya) of knowledge without them.
An attribute is essential if the essence cannot be conceived without it.
3
Apply to the Mu'tazili argument
Since commonality in the specific only forces commonality in essential general attributes, and qidam/huduth are not essential, the argument fails. The shared specific attribute (relation to a known object) does not force sharing of pre-eternity or temporality.
This is the final refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity and temporal origination are part of the essence of knowledge.
Correction: Sanusi argues they are not essential because the concept of knowledge is independent of these attributes; we can understand what knowledge is without knowing its age.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what determines whether commonality in the specific forces commonality in the general?
A) The general attribute must be essential (dhati)
B) The specific must be exactly the same
C) The general attribute must be temporal
D) The specific attribute must be unique

Key Takeaway
The true refutation of the Mu'tazili argument is that commonality in specific attributes only forces commonality in essential general attributes, and pre-eternity/temporality are not essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because you share a specific quality with someone (like intelligence or knowledge), you are essentially the same in all respects? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own assumptions about yourself and others? What would it look like to separate the essence of your knowledge from its temporal circumstances?
?
When you think about the knowledge you have, do you attach it to when or how you learned it (e.g., 'I learned this yesterday')? How does this passage call you to value knowledge for its essence rather than its age or origin? What would change in your pursuit of knowledge if you focused on its intrinsic quality rather than its novelty or pedigree?
?
If pre-eternity and temporality are not part of the essence of knowledge, then what does that imply about the knowledge you seek from God? Are you seeking knowledge as a mere accumulation of facts (accidental), or are you striving to grasp the essence of divine truths? Let this lesson push you to purify your intention in seeking knowledge—are you doing it for God's sake or for some accidental benefit?

Views on the Most Specific Attribute of God
Sanusi reports and critiques various opinions on the most specific attribute of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly opinions on the most specific attribute of God and the critiques offered by Sanusi and Ibn al-Tilimsani.

Core Idea
The search for God's most specific attribute reveals deep disagreements and subtle reasoning.

Sanusi presents a survey of opinions on what uniquely defines God. From Mu'tazilite views to al-Razi's proposal and Ibn al-Tilimsani's sharp critique, this passage shows that even learned scholars disagreed. Understanding these arguments sharpens our own theological precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the flow of opinions and critiques settle in your mind.
The passage contains multiple voices; pay attention to who is speaking.
2
Identify each opinion and its argument
List the different views mentioned: the Mu'tazila on pre-eternity, the Mu'tazila on 'state', and al-Shaykh (al-Razi) on power over invention. Note the proof al-Razi gives from the Quran.
Notice that Sanusi does not endorse any view here; he is reporting and critiquing.
3
Analyze Ibn al-Tilimsani's critique
Examine Ibn al-Tilimsani's two objections: first, that the Quranic verse only distinguishes God from creation, not states His specific attribute; second, that power over invention is not the most specific because it is a sifa ma'nawiyya dependent on the essence.
This critique shows the depth of kalam methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلف الناسُ في أخص وصْفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا : فقال قوم مِنَ المعتزلة : إنَّه القدم ، وقد سبق رده (۱) ومنهم مَنْ زعمَ : أَنَّهُ حال تُوجِبُ لهُ تعالى كونه حياً عالماً قادراً مريداً ، ولا إفصاح في هذه المقالة عن هذه الصفة ونُقِلَ عنِ الشيخ : أنَّ خاصيَّةَ الإله القدرة على الاختراع ، واختاره الفخرُ في بعض كتبه (۲) ، واحتج لهُ : بأنَّ موسى صلواتُ الله وسلامه على نبينا وعليهِ أجابَ فرعونَ لمَّا سألَهُ عن حقيقة ربِّ العالمينَ قالَ لهُ : ﴿رَبُّ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَمَا بَيْنَهُمَا ﴾ [الشعراء : ٢٤] ، فلولا أنَّ ذلك خاصية الإلهِ لَمَا كانَ الجواب لائقاً قالَ ابنُ التَّلمساني : ( ولا حُجَّةَ له في ذلكَ ؛ فإنَّ ( ما ) كما يُسأَلُ بها ويُراد بالسؤالِ فهُمُ الحقيقةِ .. قد تُطلق لطلب تمييز الحقيقة ، وما ذكره موسى عليه السلام يصلح لتمييزه تعالى عن سائر الممكنات . وقول الشيخ : ( إِنَّ تلك خاصيَّةُ الإله » لعله أراد أنَّ هذا الوصف « لا يثبتُ لغيرِ الله تعالى ردّاً على المعتزلة ؛ إِذْ تزعم أنَّ العبد يشارك الله تعالى في ذلكَ باعتبار أنَّهُ يُوجِدُ أفعالَهُ عندَهم ، ولم يُرِدْ أَنَّهُ أخص وصْفِ ذاتِهِ ؛ فإنَّ القدرة على الاختراع عندَهُ مِنْ صفاتِ المعاني التي يستدعي الاتصافُ بها تقرُّرَ الذاتِ بدونِها في العقل ، فلا تكون أخص وصْفِ الذاتِ ، وإلا لدار ذلك ، والله أعلم)
People differed concerning the most specific attribute of the Creator, exalted and majestic: a group from the Mu'tazila said: it is pre-eternity, and its refutation has preceded. And among them is one who claimed: that it is a state (ḥāl) that necessitates for Him, exalted, His being living, knowing, powerful, willing, and there is no clarity in this statement concerning this attribute. And it was transmitted from al-Shaykh: that the specific property of divinity is power over invention (al-qudra ʿalā al-ikhtirāʿ), and al-Fakhr chose it in some of his books. And he argued for it: that Moses, blessings and peace of God upon our Prophet and upon him, answered Pharaoh when he asked him about the reality of the Lord of the worlds, saying to him: 'Lord of the heavens and the earth and what is between them' [al-Shuʿarā': 24]; if that were not the specific property of divinity, the answer would not have been fitting. Ibn al-Tilimsānī said: (There is no proof for him in that; for the word 'what' (mā), just as it is asked with and intended by the question to be about the reality, it may be used to request discrimination of the reality. And what Moses, peace be upon him, mentioned is suitable for distinguishing Him, exalted, from all other contingent beings. And the statement of al-Shaykh: 'Indeed that is the specific property of divinity' — perhaps he intended that this attribute is not established for other than God, exalted, as a refutation of the Mu'tazila, since they claim that the servant shares with God in that, considering that he brings about his actions according to them. And he did not intend that it is the most specific attribute of His essence; for power over invention, according to him, is among the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) that being qualified with them requires the essence to be established in the intellect without them, so it cannot be the most specific attribute of the essence; otherwise there would be circularity. And God knows best.)
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and critique of various opinions on the most specific attribute (أخص وصف) of God. The author, Sanusi, first reports that the Mu'tazila differed: some said it is pre-eternity (القدم), but he notes that this view was already refuted earlier. Others said it is a state (حال) that necessitates God's attributes of being living, knowing, willing, etc., but this is considered unclear. Then he mentions that a certain 'Shaykh' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) held that the specific property of divinity is power over invention (القدرة على الاختراع), a view al-Razi himself chose in some of his books. Al-Razi's proof is the verse where Moses answers Pharaoh's question about the Lord by mentioning the heavens and earth and what is between them. The argument is that if that were not the specific property (خاصية) of divinity, Moses' answer would not have been appropriate. Ibn al-Tilimsani then critiques this proof on two grounds. First, he says the word 'ma' (ما) can be used to ask for a distinguishing description, not necessarily the inner reality (حقيقة). Moses' answer serves to distinguish God from other contingent beings, not to state His specific attribute. Second, he suggests that the Shaykh's statement might have been directed against the Mu'tazila who claim that a servant can create his own acts (أفعاله). The Shaykh may have meant that this property (power over invention) is exclusive to God, not that it is the most specific attribute of God's essence. Ibn al-Tilimsani explains that power over invention is among the attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني), which are attributes that require the essence to be established without them in the intellect; thus they cannot be the most specific attribute of the essence itself, otherwise a circular dependency would occur. The passage concludes with 'God knows best'.

Key Terms
أخص وصف
القدم
حال
خاصية الإله
القدرة على الاختراع
ابن التلمساني
صفات المعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the most specific attribute of God is definitively known and agreed upon.
Correction: The passage shows there is significant disagreement among scholars, and even strong proofs are critiqued. The correct position requires careful reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group claimed that the most specific attribute of God is pre-eternity (القدم)?
A) The Ash'aris
B) A group from the Mu'tazila
C) Al-Shaykh (al-Razi)
D) Ibn al-Tilimsani

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilimsani's critique shows that power over invention cannot be the most specific attribute of God because it is an attribute of meaning that depends on the essence, thus leading to circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever assume you know His 'most specific attribute' with certainty? How would your attitude change if you realized that even the great scholars (like al-Razi and Ibn al-Tilimsani) disagreed on this very point?
?
Ibn al-Tilimsani pointed out a logical flaw in a proof based on a Quranic verse. Are you willing to subject your own understanding of divine attributes to rigorous logical critique, even if it means discarding a comfortable belief?
?
The passage shows that previous arguments (like the Mu'tazila's claim of pre-eternity) can be refuted. What cherished belief about God do you hold that you have never critically examined? Will you let this lesson push you to investigate it?

The More Correct View: God's Essence is Unknown
Sanusi states the dominant view that God's essence is unknowable and lists its supporters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the majority of Ash'ari scholars hold that God's essence is unknowable, which is presented as the more correct position despite a minority view.

Core Idea
God's essence remains beyond human perception.

Sanusi begins by stating a crucial theological conclusion: if the most specific attribute of the Creator is unknown, then His essence is unknown. This lesson introduces the dominant Ash'ari position on the unknowability of God's essence and the prominent scholars who upheld it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the author's voice: 'I say' signals a personal conclusion.
The passage begins with a logical conditional: if X is unknown, then Y follows.
2
Identify the main claim and its supporting scholars
Find the claim that God's essence is unknown (مجهول) and list the four or five scholars named. Note that al-Razi is cited as a dissenter in one of his early works.
The supporting scholars are pillars of Ash'ari theology: al-Baqillani, al-Juwayni, al-Ghazali, and al-Razi.
3
Reflect on the significance of the minority view
Consider why al-Razi's early position is mentioned even though he later agreed. This shows that scholarly consensus is not absolute and that positions can evolve.
This prepared the ground for the subsidiary question discussed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وإذا تبيَّنَ لكَ أنَّ أخصَّ وصْفِ الباري جلَّ وعلا مجهول . . عرفتَ أنَّ ذاتَهُ تعالى غيرُ معروفة للبشرِ ، وهو الأصحُ مِنَ القولين وإليه ذهب القاضي وإمام الحرمين وحُجَّةُ الإسلام والإمام الفخرُ في أكثرِ كتبِهِ ، واختارَ في كتاب ( الإشارة » ـ وهو مِنْ أَوَّلِ مُصنَّفَاتِهِ - أنَّها معلومة (۳) ، وعلى المنع : فهل هو مطلقاً ولو في الآخرة ، أو إنَّما في الحال ، ويجوز أن تصير معلومة بعد ؟ نقل سيفُ الدينِ عنِ الإمامِ والغزالي المنع مطلقاً ، ونقل فيهِ الوقف عن القاضي وضرار (٤) واحتجَّ مَنْ قالَ بأنَّ حقيقةَ الذاتِ الكريمة معلومة : بما تقدَّمَ مِنْ جواب موسى عليه السلام لفرعون حين سألَهُ عن الحقيقة ، وقد سبق ردُّه ، واحتجوا أيضاً : بأنَّا نحكم على الذات العليَّةِ بأحكام ، والحكم
I say: When it becomes clear to you that the most specific attribute of the Creator, exalted and majestic, is unknown, then you know that His essence, exalted, is not known to humans, and this is the more correct of the two opinions. Al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn, Ḥujjat al-Islām, and Imām al-Fakhr in most of his books adopted it, although he chose in the book 'al-Ishārah' — which is among his earliest works — that it is known. And concerning the prohibition [of knowledge]: is it absolute, even in the Hereafter, or only in the present state, and it is possible that it could become known afterwards? Sayf al-Dīn transmitted from Imām [al-Ḥaramayn] and al-Ghazālī the absolute prohibition, and he transmitted from al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār suspension (al-waqf). And those who said that the reality of the noble essence is known argued: by what preceded of the answer of Moses, peace be upon him, to Pharaoh when he asked him about the reality, and its refutation has already preceded. And they also argued: that we judge the supreme essence with judgments, and the judgment...
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument establishing that the essence (ذات) of God is unknown to humans, which is presented as the more correct view. The author ('I say') states that if the most specific attribute of God is unknown (as concluded in the previous discussion), then the essence itself is unknown. He lists prominent scholars who held this view: al-Qāḍī (al-Bāqillānī), Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī), Ḥujjat al-Islām (al-Ghazālī), and al-Fakhr al-Rāzī in most of his books, though al-Rāzī in his early work 'al-Ishārah' held that it is known. Then the passage raises a subsidiary question: is the unknowability absolute (even in the afterlife) or only in this world with possibility of later knowledge? Sayf al-Dīn al-Āmidī transmitted from al-Juwaynī and al-Ghazālī the absolute prohibition, while from al-Qāḍī and Ḍirār he transmitted suspension (وقف), i.e., withholding judgment. The passage then presents the proof of those who claim knowability: they rely on the answer of Moses to Pharaoh (mentioned earlier) and on the fact that we make judgments about God's essence (e.g., that He is living, knowing), implying that we have some knowledge of it. However, the author notes that this refutation has already preceded (in the previous section). The text cuts off abruptly at 'والحكم' (and the judgment...). The commentary must note that this passage is incomplete in the provided OCR.

The Subsidiary Question: Absolute or Temporary Unknowability
Sanusi examines the duration of unknowability and the opposing proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether the unknowability of God's essence is absolute (even in the afterlife) or temporary, and the argument used by those who claim knowability.

Core Idea
The unknowability of God's essence is not merely a this-worldly condition.

Having established that God's essence is unknown in this life, Sanusi raises a further question: is this unknowability permanent, or may it cease in the Hereafter? He transmits the views of early scholars and then presents the proof of those who argue for knowability, noting its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the subsidiary question is introduced with 'وعلى المنع' (regarding the prohibition).
The text uses 'المنع' to refer to the denial of knowledge of God's essence.
2
Identify the two positions on duration
Find the two possibilities: absolute prohibition even in the Hereafter, or only in this life with potential later knowledge. Then note who transmitted what.
Sayf al-Dīn is important as a transmitter of these views.
3
Analyze the proof of the opponents
The passage summarizes two arguments used by those who claim the essence is known: the reply of Moses to Pharaoh, and the fact that we judge the essence with attributes. Note that the author says the refutation has already preceded.
This reminds us that the earlier discussion already addressed these proofs.

Critique of Using 'Quality' for Divine Attributes
Sanusi critiques applying the term 'quality' (kayfiyyah) to God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'quality' (kayfiyyah) is inappropriate for divine attributes and how terms like azaliyyah and abadiyyah are actually negations, not positive qualities.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not 'qualities' — they are sanctifications and negations.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his critique of the philosophical approach to divine attributes. He warns against using the term 'quality' for God's attributes because it implies change and renewal, which are impossible for the Eternal. He explains that what some thinkers call 'qualities' are really negations of non-existence and sanctifications of God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence; it may continue in the next section.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'kayfiyyah', 'azaliyyah', 'abadiyyah', 'wujub', and 'salb' in the passage. Note how they are defined or discussed.
These terms are central to understanding the critique.
3
Understand the critique and reinterpretation
The passage criticizes using 'kayfiyyah' for divine attributes because it suggests change. Then it explains that the philosophers' definition avoids deficiency but still claims qualities are real. Finally, it shows that the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) reinterpret these terms as negations and sanctifications.
Reflect on why negations are more appropriate than positive descriptions for God.

Source Text

And upon this, in the first proof also there are verbal and semantic criticisms that Sharaf al-Din, may Allah have mercy on him, pointed out. Among them: The application of 'qualities' (kayfiyyat) to some of the attributes of Allah Most High: It is a word that suggests renewal (tajaddud) and change (taghayyur), and no explanation (sharh) has been transmitted regarding it, so it is not permissible to apply it concerning Him Most High, nor to ask about it. Commentary
This passage presents a critique of using the term 'quality' (kayfiyyah) for divine attributes. The author begins by noting that Sharaf al-Din pointed out verbal and semantic objections to the first proof (the one discussed in the previous lesson). One objection is the application of 'qualities' to some of Allah's attributes. The word 'kayfiyyah' suggests change and renewal, which are inappropriate for God. No scriptural warrant exists for this term, so it should not be used for God, nor should one ask about it using that term. The author explains that the reason the Imam (maybe al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) used it is that the philosophers defined 'quality' in a way that does not imply deficiency: it is an attribute that does not require a relation to something else or division within its own essence. Some divine attributes fit this definition. However, the philosophers went further and considered qualities as real, existing meanings. The author then clarifies that what the Imam called 'quality' - such as azaliyyah (eternity without beginning), abadiyyah (eternity without end), and wujub (necessity of existence) - are actually sanctifications (taqdisat) and negations (salb) when understood by the verified scholars. For example, azaliyyah means pre-eternity, which is the negation of prior non-existence; abadiyyah means everlastingness, which is the negation of future non-existence. The passage ends mid-explanation of abadiyyah, but the key point is clear: these attributes are not positive qualities but negative descriptions (salb) that relate to the essence itself, not to a quality added to it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That terms like 'azaliyya' and 'abadiyya' are positive attributes added to God's essence.
Correction: They are actually negations of non-existence: azaliyyah negates prior non-existence, and abadiyyah negates future non-existence. They are sanctifications (taqdisat) of the essence, not positive qualities.

Key Takeaway
Attributes like azaliyyah and abadiyyah are not positive qualities but negations of non-existence, affirming God's transcendence.

Understanding 'Kayfiyya' in Divine Attributes
Sharaf al-Din's objection to using 'kayfiyya' for God and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'kayfiyya' (quality) is problematic when applied to God and how Muslim theologians resolved this by interpreting such attributes as negations.

Core Idea
Kayfiyya for God is a term that must be purged of any implication of change.

When discussing God's attributes, some philosophical terms can be misleading. Sharaf al-Din objected to using 'kayfiyya' for God because it suggests change and temporality. In this passage, the author explains the resolution: the philosophers defined 'kayfiyya' in a way that does not imply deficiency, and moreover, the attributes often labeled as 'qualities' are actually negations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. This will give you an overall sense of the discussion.
2
Identify the core objection
Notice that Sharaf al-Din objects to using 'kayfiyya' for God because it suggests change (tajaddud) and no revealed text permits it.
3
See how the philosophers' definition resolves the issue
The author explains that philosophers defined 'kayfiyya' as an attribute that does not entail a relation or division, thus removing the implication of deficiency.
4
Understand the reinterpretation of key terms as negations
Examine how 'azaliyya', 'abadiyya', and 'wujub' are explained as negations of nonexistence. For example, 'azaliyya' is the negation of prior nonexistence.
5
Reflect on the response to the counterargument
Some argue that 'wujub' is a positive quality because it affirms existence. The author replies that it affirms by negating its opposite, like saying 'no doubt.'
This shows how negation can be a valid form of affirmation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإِنْ قامَ القاطع على امتناع الشَّرْكة فيهِ عقلاً ، فهو كلي
قلنا : هذا راجع إلى اصطلاح في التسمية وتلقيبِ ، وإلا فالعلم بالتمييز في الوجودِ يستدعي امتناعَ الشِّرْكَةِ العقليَّةِ ، وقد سلَّمَ ذلك (١)
وعليه في الدليل الأول أيضاً مناقشات لفظية ومعنوية أشار إليها شرف الدين رحمه الله تعالى :
فمنها : إطلاق الكيفيَّاتِ على بعض صفاتِ اللهِ تعالى(۲) : وهو لفظ مُوهِم للتجدُّدِ والتغير ، ولم يرد به شرح ، فلا يجوز إطلاقه في حقِّهِ تعالى(۳) ، ولا يُسأَلُ به عنه ، وإنَّما حمله على إطلاقِهِ : أَنَّ الحكماء رسموا الكيفية بوجهِ لا تُوهِمُ نقصاً ، فقالوا : هي صفةٌ لا تستدعي نسبةً ولا قسمة لذاتها ، وهذا القسم مِنَ الصفاتِ كذلكَ
إلا أنَّ الفلاسفة زعموا أنَّ الكيفياتِ مِنْ أقسام المعاني الموجودة . وما سماه الإمامُ بالكيفيَّةِ مِنَ الأزليَّة والأبدية والوجوب يرجع إلى تقديسات في الذاتِ وسلب عندَ المحققين ؛ فمعنى الأزلية : هو القدم ؛ وهو سلب العدم السابق ، ومعنى الأبدية : هو البقاء ؛ وهو سلبُ العدمِ اللاحقِ ، ومعنى الوجوبِ : أَنَّهُ لا يقبلُ الانتفاء بحال ومَنِ احتج على أنَّهُ ثبوت ؛ بأنَّهُ يُؤكِّدُ بهِ الوجود ، وتأكيد الشيء تحقيقه ، والشيء لا يُحقَّقُ بنقيضه.. فجوابُهُ : أَنَّهُ يُحقَّقُ بسلْبٍ نقيضه ؛ كقولنا : هذا حق لا شك فيه ، كذا نقولُ : وجود واجبٌ ؛ أي : لا ينتفي بحالٍ
If a decisive proof is established that it is impossible for there to be a partner in it rationally, then it is universal. We said: This reverts to a convention in naming and titling; otherwise, knowledge of distinction in existence necessarily entails the rational impossibility of partnership, and that has been conceded. Upon this, the first proof also has verbal and substantive objections alluded to by Sharaf al-Din, may Allah have mercy on him: among them: applying the term 'qualities' (kayfiyyat) to some of the attributes of Allah Most High: it is a word suggestive of renewal and change, and no explanation has come (in revelation) regarding it, so it is not permissible to apply it to Him Most High, nor may He be asked about it. Rather, what compelled its use is that the philosophers defined 'quality' in a way that does not suggest deficiency; they said: it is an attribute that does not necessitate a relation (nisba) nor a division (qisma) of the essence, and this class of attributes is likewise. However, the philosophers claimed that qualities are among the categories of existing meanings. What the Imam called 'quality' from among the terms 'eternality' (azaliyya), 'everlastingness' (abadiyya), and 'necessity' (wujub) reverts, according to the verifiers, to sanctifications of the essence and negation. So the meaning of 'eternality' is pre-eternity (qidam), which is the negation of prior nonexistence; the meaning of 'everlastingness' is permanence (baqaʼ), which is the negation of subsequent nonexistence; and the meaning of 'necessity' is that it does not admit of nonexistence in any state. And whoever argues that it is a positive attribute (thubut) because it affirms existence, and the affirmation of a thing is its actualization, and a thing is not actualized by its opposite — his answer is that it is actualized by the negation of its opposite, as we say: 'This is true, no doubt about it.' Similarly we say: 'Existence is necessary,' i.e., it is not negated in any state.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage presents one of the verbal and substantive objections raised by Sharaf al-Din regarding the first proof. The objection concerns the use of the term 'kayfiyya' (quality) for some of God's attributes. The objection says this term suggests change and temporality, and since no revealed text permits it, it should not be used for God. The author responds by noting that the philosophers (presumably Muslim philosophers) defined 'kayfiyya' in a way that does not imply deficiency: it is an attribute that does not entail a relation or division. However, the philosophers considered qualities as real existing entities. The author then explains what the Imam (al-Razi? or al-Ash'ari?) meant by 'kayfiyya' regarding attributes like 'azaliyya', 'abadiyya', and 'wujub'. According to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), these terms actually refer to negations (salb) and sanctifications of the essence. For example, 'azaliyya' means pre-eternity, which is the negation of prior nonexistence; 'abadiyya' means everlastingness, the negation of subsequent nonexistence; 'wujub' (necessity) means that nonexistence is impossible in any state. The author then addresses an objection: some argue that 'wujub' is a positive attribute (thubut) because it affirms existence. The answer is that it affirms existence by negating its opposite (nonexistence), just as saying 'this is true, no doubt' affirms truth by denying doubt. So the term 'wajib' (necessary) simply means 'not nonexistent in any state'.

Correction: The philosophers define 'quality' as an attribute that does not entail relation or division, so it does not imply deficiency. Moreover, terms like 'azaliyya' are actually negations, not positive qualities.

On Naming Attributes as Relations (Idaafaat)
Discussion of whether divine attributes are mere relations or realities with relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that divine attributes are realities that possess relations, as opposed to the Mu'tazilite reduction to mere relations.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real, not mere relations—they are realities that inherently possess relations.

The text addresses a crucial theological debate: Are God's attributes nothing but relations, or are they real entities that have relations? The Ash'ari position affirms the latter, distinguishing between attributes that are themselves realities with relations and the judgments that result from those realities. This lesson examines the argument against reducing attributes to mere relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two categories of attributes according to Ash'aris.
Pay attention to the incomplete last line; the argument likely continues.
2
Identify the sectarian difference
Understand that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduced all attributes to relations, following a philosophical approach.
3
Recognize the substantive point
The debate is not merely verbal but concerns the real nature of attributes.
4
Examine the example of knowledge
The concept of knowledge is not a relation but a reality that possesses a relation.
This is the core argument against pure relationism.
5
Note the incomplete ending
The passage ends with 'wa haqiqa' suggesting a continuation; consider what might follow.

Commentary
This passage presents another objection: the classification of divine attributes as 'relations' (idaafaat). The author explains that, according to the Ash‛aris, there are two types: some attributes are realities that themselves have relations (e.g., attributes of meanings like knowledge, which are real and have relations to what is known), and others are judgments (ahkam) of established meanings that possess relations (i.e., the attributes of meanings entail relational judgments). The Mu‛tazilite Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduced all attributes to mere relations (i.e., they are nothing but relations), often following the philosophers. The author then notes that if the Imam (apparently al-Razi or similar) intended this reduction, then the debate is not merely verbal but substantive. He refers to al-Ma‛alim where the Imam stated this. The response is that the concept of knowledge, for example, in the empirical world (shahid) is not simply a relation; rather, it is a reality (haqiqa) that possesses a relation. The passage ends with 'a reality' (wa haqiqa) suggesting an incomplete continuation.

The Ash'ari Classification of Attributes: Realities and Relations
Sanusi explains the two types of Ash'ari attributes and warns against a Mu'tazilite reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theologians classify divine attributes in terms of their relation to creation, distinguishing between attributes that are real entities with relations and attributes that are judgments of meanings with relations.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, attributes are not mere relations but real entities that involve relations.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin clarifies a subtle point in the Ash'ari doctrine of attributes. The author explains that when Ash'aris speak of attributes as 'relations' (idafat), they do not mean that attributes are purely relational; rather, they distinguish between attributes that are actual realities that have relations and attributes that are judgments based on established meanings that also have relations. This classification defends the real existence of attributes while acknowledging their relational aspect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Focus on how Sanusi presents the two types of attributes.
Notice the technical terms: حقائق ذوات إضافات and أحكام لمعان.
2
Identify the two types
The passage explicitly lists two categories. Write them down in your own words. Ask: what is the difference between a 'reality with relations' and a 'judgment of a meaning with relations'?
An example: Knowledge is a reality that relates to the known; being a Creator is a judgment based on power.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite view
Sanusi mentions Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's reduction of all attributes to pure relations. Reflect on why this is not just a verbal dispute but a substantive theological error.
Consider how reducing attributes to relations might undermine their reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : تسمية الصفاتِ بالإضافاتِ : وهي عند الأشعريَّةِ : إمَّا حقائق ذوات إضافاتٍ ، أو أحكام لمعانٍ ثابتة ذواتِ إضافات ، وقد ردَّها إلى الإضافات أبو الحسين البصري مِنَ المعتزلة ، وهو كثيراً ما ينهج منهج الفلاسفة ، فإنْ أرادَ الإمامُ ذلكَ فالكلامُ معَهُ لا يرجعُ إلى مُجرَّدِ مناقشة لفظيَّةٍ ، بل في مؤاخذة معنوية
Among them: naming the attributes as relations (idafat). And they are, according to the Ash'aris: either realities that have relations, or judgments of established meanings that have relations. Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of divine attributes as 'relations' (idafat). The author states that according to the Ash'aris, attributes are of two types: (1) realities that have relations (e.g., the attribute of knowledge is a real entity but involves a relation to the known), and (2) judgments of established meanings that have relations (e.g., the attribute of being a Creator is a judgment based on the meaning of power). The Mu'tazilite Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduced all attributes to pure relations, following the philosophical method. The author warns that if the Imam (referring to a previous scholar) meant this reduction, then the disagreement is not merely verbal but a substantive error in understanding the nature of God's attributes.

Misconception: All divine attributes are merely relational and have no reality in themselves.
Correction: The Ash'aris maintain that some attributes are real entities (like knowledge) and others are judgments based on real meanings; they all involve relations but are not reducible to them.

The Reality of Knowledge and the Witnessed Ladder
Author defends unchangeable reality of knowledge against reductionist view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge cannot be reduced to a mere relation and how the witnessed world serves as a valid analogy for the unseen.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a real entity with a relation, not just a relation—and it stays the same for God and creation.

This lesson delves into a key argument in Islamic theology: can we truly say God 'knows' in the same sense that we know? The author defends the position that the core reality of knowledge is unchangeable, using the method of analogy from the witnessed. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's view
The passage begins by referencing a view that reduces attributes to pure relations. What does that mean? Note that the author is responding to someone who argued that divine attributes are mere relations, not real entities.
The opponent is likely Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who held that knowledge is a relation.
2
Identify the author's counter-argument
Look for the phrase 'معقولُ العلم مثلاً الشاهدِ لا يرجع إلى نسبةٍ بل هو حقيقةٌ ذاتُ نسبةٍ'. This is the core claim: knowledge in the witnessed world is a reality that possesses a relation, not just a relation.
The Arabic uses 'haqiqa dhatu nisba' – a reality with a relation.
3
Apply the analogy from the witnessed
The author uses the witnessed world as a ladder (sullam) to ascend to the unseen. But he insists on perfection and transcendence. Ask yourself: how does this justify that divine knowledge must share the same reality as human knowledge?
The analogy requires that the core meaning be the same, while imperfections are negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد صرَّحَ بذلك في ( المعالم ) ، فيُقال له : معقولُ العلم مثلاً الشاهدِ لا يرجع إلى نسبةٍ بل هو حقيقةٌ ذاتُ نسبةٍ ، وحقيقة العلم لا تختلفُ بالقدم والحدوثِ وكثرة المتعلقاتِ وقلَّتِها ، فكيف يثبت على وجهِ يخالف حقيقته في الشاهدِ ، والشاهدُ سُلَّمْ يُرتقى بهِ إلى إثباتِ الحقائقِ غائباً على وجه الكمال والتنزيه ؟!
And he stated that in al-Ma'alim. So it is said to him: The concept of knowledge, for example in the witnessed [world], is not reducible to a relation; rather it is a reality possessing a relation. And the reality of knowledge does not differ due to pre-eternity or temporality, or the multiplicity or paucity of its objects. So how can it be established in a manner contrary to its reality in the witnessed, while the witnessed is a ladder through which one ascends to establish the realities of the unseen, in a manner of perfection and transcendence?
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage is a response to someone (probably Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) who reduces attributes to pure relations. The author first notes that the Imam explicitly stated his position in al-Ma'alim. Then he argues: the concept of knowledge, for example as we understand it in the witnessed world, is not merely a relation; rather it is a real entity that possesses a relation. The essence of knowledge remains the same whether it is pre-eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith), and whether it has many objects of knowledge or few. Therefore, it is impossible that God's knowledge be something completely different from what we know as knowledge. The witnessed world is a ladder to understand the unseen, but with the necessary perfection and transcendence (i.e., we affirm the same reality while negating imperfections). Hence, knowledge cannot be a pure relation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the meaning of knowledge changes between God and creation.
Correction: The author holds that the core reality is the same; only perfection differs.

Checkpoint

A) Because relations cannot be attributed to God
B) Because knowledge in the witnessed is a reality with a relation, and that reality does not change with pre-eternity or temporality
C) Because knowledge is only a relation in the unseen
D) Because the witnessed is not a reliable guide

Key Takeaway
The reality of knowledge is a consistent entity across divine and human domains, differing only in perfection, not in essence.

Is your understanding of God's attributes built on a careful analogy that preserves transcendence, or have you fallen into either denial or anthropomorphism? Examine the state of your heart: does it tremble at the thought that He knows you truly and intimately?

Why Sunni Scholars Avoid Saying Attributes Are 'Other Than' the Essence
Author explains why Sunni scholars avoid saying attributes are 'other than' the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians refrain from saying that God's attributes are 'other than' His essence, even though they affirm a conceptual distinction.

Core Idea
Sunni scholars avoid saying attributes are 'other than' Allah's essence because it wrongly suggests separability.

In this lesson, Sanusi warns against careless theological language. The Sunni position on attributes is that they are neither identical to nor separate from the essence, but using the word 'other' (al-ghayr) can mislead. He explains why the imams avoid this term and dismisses objections based on verbal quibbles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the key term 'mughayira' and how it is avoided.
The passage is short but packed with theological caution.
2
Identify the avoided phrase and its implication
Find the Arabic phrase 'anna sifatihi ta'ala mughayira li-dhatihi' and understand why the imams refrain from it. The implication of possible separation (mufaraqa) is the primary concern.
The phrase 'other than' might sound harmless but can be misunderstood by common people.
3
Understand the two reasons for avoidance
Sanusi gives two reasons: 1) it implies to others the possibility of separation, and 2) the revealed law has not permitted this exact wording. Reflect on why theological precision matters.
The second reason shows that Sunni theology is grounded in scriptural language, not philosophical speculation.
4
Reflect on the dismissal of objections
The author dismisses these as 'verbal objections' (i'tiradat lafdhiyya). The meaning is correct but the wording is avoided. This highlights a key principle: avoid misleading expressions even if the concept is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : إطلاقُهُ أَنَّ صفاتِهِ تعالى مغايرة لذاتِهِ : وأئمةُ السَّنَّةِ رضوان الله تعالى عليهم ممتنعونَ مِنْ إطلاقِ ذلكَ ؛ لما يُوهِمُ الغيرِ مِنْ صحَةِ المفارقة ، ولم يردِ الشرع بإطلاقه ، فلا يصح هذه الاعتراضات اللفظية
Among them: his assertion that His attributes are other than (mughayira) His essence. And the imams of the Sunni school, may Allah be pleased with them, refrain from asserting that, because it implies to others the possibility of separation, and the revealed law has not permitted its assertion. So these verbal objections are not valid.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of whether it is correct to say that God's attributes are 'other than' (mughayira) His essence. The author criticizes someone who uses such wording. The Sunni imams avoid this expression because it might suggest that the attributes are separable from the essence, which is not the case. Moreover, the Shari'a has not used this exact wording. Therefore, the author dismisses the objections against the Sunni position as mere verbal quibbling, since the meaning is correct (attributes are not the same as the essence) but the precise expression is avoided to prevent misunderstanding.

Key Terms
المغايرة (al-mughayara)
الذات (al-dhat)
الصفات (al-sifat)
المفارقة (al-mufaraqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Sunni theologians deny any distinction between God's attributes and His essence, because they avoid saying attributes are 'other than' the essence.
Correction: In fact, they affirm a real conceptual distinction but avoid the term 'other' because it can imply separability, and they stick to scriptural language.

Checkpoint

C) Because they reject any distinction between essence and attributes
D) Because they consider attributes to be purely relational

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position avoids saying attributes are 'other' because it falsely suggests separability, not because they deny a conceptual distinction.

The Objection Regarding the Wording 'Attributes Are Distinct from Essence'
Sanusi refutes the objection that attributes are distinct from essence by noting the salaf avoid that wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Imams of the Sunnah avoid the wording that attributes are distinct from essence and how Sanusi refutes the objection based on that.

Core Idea
Words matter: the Salaf avoid wording that implies separation of attributes from essence.

Sanusi addresses an objection about the phrase 'attributes are distinct from essence.' The objector claims that using such wording is problematic. Sanusi explains that the Salaf Imams deliberately avoid this wording because it can be misunderstood to mean the attributes can separate from the essence. Since Islamic law does not authorize this exact wording, the objection fails. Let's examine the passage carefully.

Guided Path

3

Sanusi responds by stating that the Imams of the Sunnah refrain from using that wording. Why? Because it suggests to others the possibility of separation (a physical or temporal separation), which is not intended. Also, the Law (Qur'an and Sunnah) does not use this exact wording.
4
